		<shortDescription>Penetrating Psychological Study</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>8 out of 10 Sy is one of those everyday type of guys you see around and then completely forget. Yet everyone DOES have a life even if for some it is only an inner life. For Sy it is a typical suburban family who he has known for years due to the fact that he develops there pictures. His obsession with them grows until it spills over with frightening results. Sounds like the basic setup for another psycho/stalker scenario and yet it isn't. This film far exceeds that and in many ways takes it into a whole new level. At times this film seems much more like a drama or even a sad movie. You actually feel for this Sy guy. He is still threatening, but in a much more complex way. The director does a very good job at getting you into Sy's emotional state. The color schemes and music bring you into Sy's world and makes for a lasting impression. The nightmare sequence is especially well done. Williams, as Sy, is terrific. His venture into unchartered territory has proved to be a successful one. His comic persona has always had a sort of 'lonely boy looking for attention' type quality. He creates a character that has a dark side and yet still shows a lot of humanity. He gets the viewer to become sensitive with someone they might not otherwise want to. This creates good tension as the viewer then becomes torn between liking him and fearing him. If there is anything wrong with what is otherwise an outstanding effort is the fact that it seems to be too rehearsed and neatly packaged. It's point is too bombarded and there seems to be no opening for a more free form style. The stylization is strong and at times drowns out the reality of the films main message. Yet on the whole director Romanek shows a lot of promise. He respects his characters and doesn't try to exploit them. His script is intelligent and dark and yet still tasteful and restrained. His filming and choosing of the young actor who plays the child is good example of this. He is not overly cute or precocious. He shows the natural awkwardness of a typical child and yet still conveys a perception to things that the adults don't. He is a child as they really are.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-07-30</date>
		<username>Dan Heller (argv@danheller.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>great film for its qualities, despite my desire for more character development</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo is a thriller where Robin Williams plays a psycho with a conscience. It's a nicely paced, intelligent suspense film, where its qualities lie on aesthetic qualities, such as pace, soundtrack, and other dramatic elements. This is not a movie that relies on surprise, shock, or intrigue.The plot revolves around Robin Williams' character, Sy Parrish, a lonely and introverted photo lab technician at a SavMart convenience store. He lives his life vicariously through the photos of the regulars who drop off their film to be processed. One of his quips in a long series of narratives interjected throughout the movie, reminds us that, `people take pictures of happy times in their lives. No one ever takes a picture of something they want to forget.' The happier he perceives other people, the more he withdraws from his own life and seeks to be a part of theirs. But it's a special family, the Yorkins - a mother, father and son -- that he most strongly bonds to over the past nine years. He fantasizes that he is their `Uncle Sy', and in his mind, they treat him as such. As his emotional stability becomes increasingly unbalanced, he finally snaps when he discovers a secret that could tear apart the family.What carries the film is the character profiles and excellent visual qualities. The characters reflect their environment: cold, stark, and generically plain. Physical spaces are large, vast areas of empty isles, huge rooms, fluourescently lit sterile environments - just like the people. In a sense, nothingness. Yet, there is a lot underneath. And this is where the film's lesser quality comes in.The characters lack is a sense of depth. Psychotics are more interesting when we know their history - we don't know anything about Sy's past, and the notion that he has none takes away more than it adds. There are other conflicts that go untested, which also leaves a sense of incompleteness. (We don't need `resolution', just a sense that an issue is addressed.) For all the empty vastness of the movie that symbolizes Sy's profile, it's a perfect time to add a tiny drop of black paint on a vast white canvas: show something that gives us history. Many of Sy's pivotal plot-turning acts are done more for symbolic effect rather than to guide the plot along, and are therefore left with question-marks over the audience's collective head. Why would he do certain things? He's supposed to be cold and calculating, but some of his actions are anything but.One Hour Photo is almost more of an artsy film than a mainstream one, and in that genre, these concerns really needn't be addressed to satisfy its target audience. In the end, I loved the film, and all its qualities, despite my desire for more character development.This is Robin Williams' third film of 2002, all of which feature him as a psychotic. But it's this role that is probably the most dramatic and believable. What makes it even better is that you almost forget it's him soon after the movie begins. For an actor as easily type-cast as Williams, this marks a notable step forward in the breadth of his work.The movie was written and directed by Mark Romanek, most known for music videos; most prominently, Madonna's 1999 video collection. This is his first film, unless you want to count his indie-effort, Static, in 1985, which didn't get very much attention. In One Hour Photo, Romanek uses techniques very reminiscent of Stanley Kubrik - with the ultra-wide-angle symmetry shots, long, quiet scenes, and dramatic pauses between lines to achieve a sense of suspense and psychological drama.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>There is nothing more dangerous than a familiar face.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is the most disturbing movie I feel I have ever seen, but boy is it a good movie as well! I am a fan of actors turning themselves into versatile performers, as you get to see them in many different ways. That is what Robin Williams is doing with his career right now and a smart move if you ask me. ?One Hour Photo' will make you think long and hard about the people that serve behind a shop counter, especially at your local photo developer.In ?One Hour Photo', Seymour "Sy" Parrish is a quiet, consummately professional photo lab technician employed in a huge, impersonal shopping centre. One customer, Nina Yorkin is unaware that Sy has been carefully printing off extra sets of her family snapshots for years, adding them to a photo collage he has assembled in his apartment. Just how far will the lonely Sy go to become part of Nina's seemingly perfect American family unit? This film was written and directed by Mark Romanek. I also love the great tension that Mark included in the film, through Sy's character throughout the movie, especially at the end of the film. As he was in ?Insomnia', Williams is cast against type in ?One Hour Photo', again being the bad guy. The Yorkin Family, Nina (Connie Nielsen), Will (Michael Vartan) and Jake (Dylan Smith) are a family that Parrish knows a lot about. This is another creepy side to his character, as he has memorised the Yorkin family's address and every other minor detail, which is truly scary. Then when Sy has the chance to uncover a big secret in the Yorkin familles seamlessly perfect life, he takes advantage of his job, by using photos brought in by one of Sy's many customers, that being Maya Burson (Erin Daniels), to do so. Then the way Parrish meddles in this predicament was a truly enthralling moment of the movie for me. Throughout the movie, we see Parrish being both investigated and integrated by Detective Van Der Zee (former ER star Eriq LaSalle), who finds Sy to be a big problem, who is difficult to handle. But enough about the plot, I will leave some of it for you to learn, so you can enjoy the richness of the movie experience. I must say I appreciated the work of cinematographer Jeff Cronenweth. I especially love the ?savmart' locations on the film and the different camera angles that Cronenweth shows us in that place, as I feel they jump out at you. There is also a very tainted look to different parts of the film, again Cronenweth's influence, which is understandable, considering we are dealing with photography and a sick man in the film. Robin Williams also looks quite nasty in this film, and he was helped to look this way by the make-up department, headed by Suzanne Rodier, who did a great job in making Williams look as menacing as he could be. I really like this movie, in particular Robin William's gripping performance that is in it. ?One Hour Photo' has been compared to the brilliant ?American Beauty', which is a big rap, and I can see where people are forming that opinion from. I have a friend who works at one of my town's local photo shops and I have recommended that he watches this film, as I am sure he could relate to the character of Seymour and what he goes through in vocational sense at the photo shop. I just hope he is not like him, which I am sure he is not. This film will make us appreciate how memorable photos can be for us all, and that obsessed people can be extremely disturbing for the community that we live in. I am certain they are much closer than we all think. Perhaps you might be served by someone like that someday? My advice to you is, keep on the look out for them! CMRS gives ?One Hour Photo': 4.5 (Very Good ? Brilliant Film) </fullReview>

Too bad, cuz some better plotting would have made this a great, classic thriller.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Good Psycho-drama, Great Acting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'One Hour Photo' belongs to the category of memorable movies bringing still photography into the moving image art. Antonioni's 'Blow-up' is the masterpiece of the genre. It also belongs to the psychological drama, with the main character reminding the un-forgetable 'Psycho' character, with a striking similarity in re-creating the motivation of a criminal mind under the appearances of a quiet and kind person. The film is very well directed and filmed, but it is first of all a Robin Williams masterpiece of acting. Williams is fabulous in bringing to life the lonely Sy, his dreams of being part of a mid-class urban family, his affection to them which turns into a wrong obsession because it does not fit with the society norms. His acting is so good that the rest of the characters are rather reduced to sketches, and this may be one of the few flaws of this movie. In a year dominated in the by fantasy movies and musical, a real strong drama is refreshing. 'One Hour Photo' is one of my favorite American films of the year.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-04-23</date>
		<username>suffer-smart-magnet</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Life of the Lonely - *Minor Spoilers*</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>OHP looks into the life of Sy (Robin Williams), a 1-hour photo specialist. Over several years time, Sy becomes obsessed with his regular customers, the Yorkins. Sy learns more about them by talking to them and by covertly examining their developed photos. Sy yearns for the Yorkins' life (as portrayed through the smiling faces on their photos) so much that he starts making more and more effort to be a part of it. When Sy learns of Mr. Yorkins adultery, he begins to spiral further out of touch with reality.ACTING: Robin Williams makes one of his most notable performances in portraying Sy as asexual, perpetually reserved, and quirky. Gary Cole (Bill Owens) is good as the impersonal micro-manager who ends up firing Sy, without even knowing what Sy's job is. Eriq La Salle (Detective James Van Der Zee), like his 'E.R.' television series performance (as Dr. Peter Benton), comes across with strong presence, making a good detective.PLOT: The beginning of OHP has a slow building in tension where you aren't sure where things are going, and sometimes you feel a bit bored. As the plot unfolds, things ramp up in a very natural progression, and we never feel that the tension has been artificially pumped up. We feel for Sy in his attempts to have people in his life, even though he contrived some of them. Even though some plot points were predictable, you never felt that things were staged just to keep them unpredictable. Two plot points failed (in a minor way). When Sy is fired, he is allowed to work out the rest of the week. Unfortunately, that doesn't happen anymore, not even with long-term employees like Sy. In another minor misfire, Sy's ending reveals his abuse as a child as his motivation for his involvement with the Yorkins. It would have worked much better to put clues along the way so we could see Sy's character develop rather than springing it on us in the end.CINEMATOGRAPHY: Excellent use of color (and lack thereof) give most of the movie the stark, lonely feel that mirrors how Sy feels. At the end of a workday, Sy walks alone through an empty parking lot lit by yellow-orange sodium lights that become almost green for the camera.MUSIC: The music added well to the theme and pace of the movie.CONCLUSION: OHP is an unconventional thriller which tries with good success to open the mind of someone who is out of touch with reality. I liked the movie and would watch it again.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>mashby (me@mashby.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Although the movie is a bit stylized and somewhat sterile in it's storytelling, it's a very engaging movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm a big fan of Mark Romanek's work as a video director. I think he's a lighting genius and his hyper reality style makes the artist look ultra-perfect. Mark bring this artistic style to his directorial debut. Although I'm a big fan of it, I did find it a bit distracting in a movie format. According to the director's commentary on the DVD, his intention was to make the movie somewhat hyper realistic, but everything is just a bit too squeaky clean.Yet, this fits the story somewhat, in that the characters are somewhat stereotypical. The dialog is fairly good, but in some of the more deeper sections, the dialog is a bit lacking. The relationship between the Mrs. Yorkin (Connie Nielsen) and the Mr. Yorkin (Michael Vartan, of Alias fame) is one of the weaker points of the movie. They simply didn't have much in the way of chemistry and their relationship wasn't clearly established. You know that they have problems in their relationship, but it's vague and un-authentic.The standout performance has to go to Robin Williams. What was most impressive about his work in this film was his ability to condense himself into such a quiet and contained character. We all know him as this over-the-top extrovert and in this role he is supposed to blend into the background, which he does. When he blows his gasket, which you know he's going to do, you imagination just runs wild. He's such a lonely character in such need for some type of contact that you just feel for him.Another performance that surprised me was that of Dylan Smith, who plays the Yorkin's son Jake. His role wasn't all that large, but he really did well in it. You could tell he was really listening when he was in scene and he did a lot of small movements, like playing with a toy somewhat distractedly, that just seemed so natural. This was his first role in a movie, but you wouldn't know it from watching him work.Although the movie is a bit stylized and somewhat sterile in it's storytelling, it's a very engaging movie. That coupled with the lighting and other eye candy is why I gave it a rating of 8.</fullReview>

		<username>bas rutten</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Really gets to you</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"One Hour Photo" was a movie that really got to me. It's one of the first thrillers I've ever seen where I actually got the idea "hey, wait a minute, this could really happen".Instead of the bad guy being some kind of ultra-creepy serial killer that kills and/or rapes women in the most sick and twisted ways imaginable, the bad guy in this movie is an unattractive and lonely photo shop employee who becomes obsessed with the "happy family" for which he regularly develops photos. Although this character, played brilliantly by Robin Williams (didn't know he had it in him) is seriously disturbed, you can't help but feel sorry for him: he tries very hard but nobody likes him. That's about the first time ever I felt sorry for the bad guy. In terms of plot, nothing really spectacular ever happens. There's no gore, no murders and hardly any violence. Instead, we get a finely constructed tale of how Sy (the bad guy) slowly but steadily slips away into desperation until he finally snaps. This really got under my skin, which is something that not a lot of movies manage to do. All in all, a very finely crafted movie with lots of attention to detail and a very disturbing sense of realism. *** out of **** stars</fullReview>

I enjoyed the symbolism used throughout the film, especialy the use of lighting, colors and cleaniness all obvious references to the motif of photo development. An example, are the primary colors of the films which I believe represented the emotional state at the time such as red (warmth/rage), blue (depressed, green (envy), white (purity/innocence).As a character study the movie is interesting, but the movie has so many plot holes, coincidences, subplots that go no where and suspension of disbelief that the film disolves into the catagory of "interesting but could have been so much more" type of film. 		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Robin Williams gives a fully convincing performance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw "One Hour Photo" in the theater several months ago, with an audience that seemed to, at first, interpret the role as Robin Williams downplaying his typical brand of comedy. That tone changed as the film progressed. As written and directed by Mark Romanek (who's done visually sumptuous music videos for Nine Inch Nails, among others), I was expecting a bit more in terms of visual style--the look of the film seemed glossy and clean, yet flat and dull. I recently gave the movie another look and better interpreted Romanek's intentions. Firstly, his portrait of Sy Parrish (Williams), a lonely film developer who uses the prints of one family, the Yorkins, to give his life some semblance of meaning, is very convincing--this is the first of Williams' 'dark' roles that don't convey the actor standing on a pedestal, yelling: "I'M ROBIN WILLIAMS AND I'M DOING A DARK ROLE!" Credit the controlled dramatic layering of Williams' performance--restrained, put-upon, and hollow, he disappears in the role and makes it believable. Romanek's script is conventional save for a few dramatic twists near the end, but his attention to lighting and bright (or drab) colors to create mood is impressive. Overall, "One Hour Photo" is a diverting little item buoyed by Williams' quietly creepy performance. It's one of the better films of 2002.7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2003-03-16</date>
		<username>alansmithee_1</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Pretty much "picture" perfect</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>**SPOILER ALERT FOR PARAGRAPHS 5 AND 6**"One Hour Photo" is one impressive movie. I honestly can't remember any other film I've seen that managed to be both creepy and deeply affecting at the same time. Once I got past "Hey, it's Robin Williams with a bleached crew-cut!", I was completely immersed in his performance as Seymour ("Sy") Parrish. It's a tour de force for Williams, and proof positive for the final 3 skeptics that he can be just as brilliant in dramatic roles as he can in comic-genius mode. Even before the traumatic roots of his obsession are revealed, Sy is a perfect-pitch blend of goosebump fodder and heartrending pathos. He's a stalker, you can't help but shudder, yet at the same time you can't hate him. And the soulless, caged-animal look in his eyes when he's caught in the police cruiser's headlights seemed to perfectly crystallize his character: lonely, desperate, crying out for some connection to life beyond the safe but soul-crushing banality of the photo counter at Sav-Mart.Thematically, the film offers a scathing indictment of the voyeuristic thread running through modern American society. The image of Sy settling down to watch TV, while his montage of Yorkin family photos fills an entire wall in the background, captures this theme most powerfully. As in Hitchcock's masterful "Rear Window," director Mark Romanek forces viewers to recognize their own role as armchair voyeurs. Through the medium of a (movie) camera lens, we too observe and judge the emptiness of Sy's existence and the disintegration of his fragile hold on reality.Carrying along this camera metaphor is Romanek's stunning cinematography. It evokes Stanley Kubrick (especially in the nightmare sequence) and M. Night Shyamalan with meticulous emphasis on symmetry and perspective lines...not to mention painstaking use of color as an arc into the different characters. Sy is painted in neutral, lifeless tones (not only his clothing, but also his apartment and even his car), in contrast to the Yorkins, who are saturated with bold, vibrant hues. Standout moments in this category include the psychedelic, puke-coloured cast of the parking-lot when Sy discovers the crack in his windshield, and subtler strokes like the blood-red chair in the otherwise stark-white interrogation room.Some commentators have criticized the film for a lack of plot and "paper-thin" performances from the supporting cast. I agree that anyone who rents "OHP" looking for a plot-driven movie is bound to be disappointed. It's not about plot; it's an intensive character study, the same sort of approach Shakespeare used in "Macbeth." Even when the spotlight isn't directly trained on Sy, it's still generally reflecting some aspect of his character -- for example the scene of Will and Nina Yorkin's argument, which establishes the critical difference between Sy's idealized vision and the reality of a strained marriage. Within this "one-man show" framework, I though the supporting cast did just fine. The only one that jarred a little was Gary Cole, and that was only because I couldn't stop seeing his previous, insufferable turn as a boss in "Office Space." (I kept waiting for him to say "Yeahhhhh... I'm, ahhh...gonna have to, ahhhh...get you to go ahead and ahhhh...move your desk, Sy...")Other shortcomings were few and minor. **SPOILER ALERT** Sy's revelation of his childhood trauma seemed a little out of sync to me. It was an insightful, perhaps obligatory, scene (and another magnificent piece of acting by Williams), but it might have seemed less obtrusive if presented earlier on in the film, rather than at the end as a typical sort of "oh yeah, and by the way, this is why I'm such a head case." But that's nitpicking, really. The only other thing that troubled me was Jake's complacency in talking to this man, barely more than a stranger, who freakishly pops up at his soccer game. I kept wondering, "Didn't anybody streetproof this kid? *I'd* sure as hell have told my parents when I got home!"I don't feel this movie was too predictable. The ambiguity of what happened to the pictures from Sy's shocking "photo session" plainly frustrated some, but I found it intriguing. The other ambiguity I liked was how the film blurred the lines between heroes and villains. The fact that Will Yorkin survives in the end *did* come as a surprise, but it wasn't inconsistent with Sy's character. Either outcome would have fit, and I felt this choice had more power because forcing Will to live with the shame of his infidelity was the greater punishment. The scene of his homecoming was an exquisite and devastating example (not the film's first or only, by any means) of how much one can convey on film without words...and stalwart proof that the supporting characters had more to do than just play backdrop to Sy's chilling psychodrama. **END SPOILER ALERT**The film is so richly textured that I could go on for pages more, but I'll wrap up just as I came in: "OHP" is an extraordinary picture. It forces us to examine that squeamish area of the psyche that no one wants to admit exists (much less to visit!), implicates us in the act of voyeurism, and at least for this viewer, leaves an indelible impression. Hats off to all, especially Williams and Romanek!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>(mgreene@az.rmci.net)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Can you say Taxi Driver set in Wal-Mart?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo is a winding, artsy, cinematic mosaic of a movie that invariably succeeds on certain elemental levels while it fails on others. On the positive, the writing is for the most part original, clever, and witty - especially the narratives and how they metaphorically relate life experiences and photography. The set design was very well planned, appropriate, and the color contrasts are extremely visual and appealing. The editing is quite good based on the pacing of the movie, as there really aren't any obvious slow parts and the story transitions smoothly.However, on the whole the acting is inconsistent with Robin Williams and Gary Cole noticeably outclassing the rest of the lead cast. Additionally, the plot line is limited as the movie really doesn't make the viewer draw their own conclusions about what is happening. It is contrived and lacking sub plot. Let me expand, the basic premise of the characters is rather weak. They don't seem to think for themselves. Instead, the focus is on stimulating the viewers' sense of compassion for main character. A tragic hero, oh how sad - yawn. On top of that - the score is annoying, redundant, and particularly over-used. The ending is disappointing as well, for the most part being anti-climatic.In summary, the movie is a bit of an oddity - walking a fine line between artsy, independent film and a cliché Hollywood blockbuster. One Hour Photo should not be dismissed as rubbish or held up on a pedestal as a masterpiece. It falls somewhere in between, succeeding in some aspects while falling short in others. 6/10</fullReview>

		<date>2003-03-12</date>
		<username>Dan Franzen (dfranzen70)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Matte finish, glossy content</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For most of us, dropping off film to the one-hour photolab isn't a big deal. We drop it off, pick up our little claim ticket, and then go shopping for an hour or so. But for the photo technician, who sees hundreds and hundreds of daily life pictures, each snapshot is a glimpse into another person's world, whether it's a birthday party for an eight-year-old boy or softcore porn for a local entrepreneur. Seymour Parrish (Robin Williams) has been developing photos at the SavMart for many, many years. He's cheerful and friendly to his customers (and he does consider them to be "his" customers), and he knows his stuff when it comes to the technical aspects of the job. See, Sy's no mere clerk, although that's how you and I and most other people see him; he's a qualified, experienced technician. So much so that he gets into a brief shouting match with the guy who delivers Agfa developing equipment over a minor discrepancy in the machine.Sy lives alone; he's middleaged and has no friends or close family. He doesn't even have a dog. What he does have, though, is the opportunity to live his life through the pictures his customers have taken, from birthday parties to sex games to vacations to any and all events in people's lives.There's one family in particular through which Sy lives vicariously the most often: Nina and Will Yorkin, with son Jake (age 8). Sy's been developing the Yorkins' photos sinec before Jake was born, and he's been able to watch the boy grow.This might seem to be a normal obsession, right? At least as normal as obsessions can be, anyway. But Sy, as you may have guessed, is a little different. He has an entire wall in his house devoted to the Yorkin family. Over the past decade or so, whenever he's developed prints for Mrs. Yorkin, Sy has made a copy for himself. As a result, there are thousands and thousands of photos on the wall from the Yorkin family. When the store manager (Gary Cole) discovers the discrepancy between the number of prints produced and the number that have been actually paid for, he confronts Sy, and the downward spiral begins. Sy's obsession with his surrogate family intensifies as he descends even deeper into madness.But he's not just a creepy loner, although that's a big part of his character makeup. Sy feels a certain kinship with the family - at least with Mrs. Yorkin and Jake - and also feels protective of them. So when he comes across some damaging photos, that descent into madness accelerates rapidly.Is this a good role for Williams? In his recent turn in Insomnia, he was creepy and not at all the hyperkinetic funnyman we all know, and he was very effective at essaying deep emotions. The same holds true here. In fact, I think this is among the best work Williams has ever turned in. He's mesmerizing as Sy, making him not into an unfeeling monster but rather into an obsessive man with a warped sense of values. The other actors are well cast, too, especially Gary Cole as his boss and Connie Nielsen as Nina Yorkin. But it's Williams's show, and while in olden days he might have turned this into a tour-de-force, pull-out-the-stops performance, in One Hour Photo Williams takes a very effective subtle approach. He underplays Sy just enough to let the audience know what he's feeling and what his motivations are without beating them over the head with it.The story moves along very well, and the film is exceptionally photographed (so to speak). The original music by Reinhold Heil and Johnny Klimek is fantastic and - best of all - appropriate to the movie, which is a rare treat.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Jacob Rosen (bix171@comcast.net)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Instantly Forgettable</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Unchallenging material and execution renders this Robin Williams thriller instantly forgettable. Everything is anticipated, even the twists; there are absolutely no shocks and though the film does get a bit creepy toward the end, it does so in a voyeuristic fashion designed only to sate the viewer's appetite for torture and manipulation. Williams stars as a mild-mannered film developer at a discount retailer who develops an unhealthy fixation on a family he perceives to be perfect; when he discovers they're not, his balance is thrown and he feels he must remedy the situation. This is the kind of character you always fear manic comedian Robin Williams will play: a character self-consciously trying not to be manic comedian Robin Williams, an introspective, lonely drone who shelters his thoughts of violence behind his placid but friendly demeanor; naturally, in such a self-serving performance, he's dismal, never allowing himself to get lost in the role and revealing precious few nooks or crannies to peek into. (Which is a shame because he was much more interesting doing serious work in his prior role in `Insomnia'; at least there he was able to bring some humanity to his goading blackmailer/killer.) Mark Romanek, the writer/director, has created an cold, sterile world for Williams to roam around in that by itself is a dead giveaway but he himself brings no fresh ideas to his project and prefers to deal with everything in a precise, technical manner; everything has to be timed perfectly (including the score by the great Reinhold Heil and Johnny Klimak from `Run Lola Run') with no margin for error. Unfortunately, it makes for a very uninteresting movie. There is, however, some fine tracking work from cinematographer Jeff Cronenweth, who also did `K-19: The Widowmaker' and `Fight Club'.</fullReview>

		<username>binaryg</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A Subversive Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mark Romanek has written and directed a disquieting, subtle, and excellent film in One Hour Photo. The tagline at IMDb "There's nothing more dangerous than a familiar face," could read, "There's nothing more disturbing than a familiar place." Mr. Romanek has created a disturbing film populated by people we can recognize moving through the space Americans find themselves moving every day. (At one point while Sy is spying on the Yorkins, his eyes are the rearview mirror of his car.) Using color, pacing, sound and excellent acting Romanek creates a subversive and unsettling portrait of our cultural neurosis. The most disturbing part of the film for me is how alienated the people are from one another and from their environment. The Savmarts of our world oppress us. Sy isn't the only lonely character here and he doesn't destroy Yorkin family, the cultural of meaningless terminal greed pictured here has done that quite nicely.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-03-04</date>
		<username>David Saliba (dsaliba-1)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Original, well crafted, and well acted</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The first order of business is to declare that Robin Williams' performance is superb. As far as I'm concerned, this is Williams' best work to date. The second order of business is to point out what initially disturbed me as I was viewing the film for the first time. I felt that the pacing in the beginning was rather slow. But after thinking about it and discussing it with another viewer, I have come to the conclusion that the pacing cleverly supports the main character's persona and reinforces (emphasizes) that character's predicament in life. So the pacing (though at first disturbing) works very well.The cinematography in a number of silent scenes is carefully planned and deliberately symbolic (lots of echoings of photography). In some talking scenes there are "photography" metaphors and historical expositions. Though interesting, these scenes are sometimes slightly intrusive. But overall, I think they help support the careful character development so central to the plot.The script is well written and carries the plot exquisitely to its "terrifying" conclusion. And as it turns out, the reason that the conclusion is so terrifying (Williams' superb acting is a given, of course) is because the pacing, the cinematography, and the metaphors distract the viewer (derail, so to speak) just enough to allow the ending to erupt with such unexpected elegance that the viewer is stunned by the final "meaning" -- the truth being told by this fascinating tale. The "truth," as it turns, out is pretty terrifying.</fullReview>
	</review>

This was true for the main characters in this story.This is the type of movie that may seem boring to some people because it appeals to people who like a story about different types of people, the complexity of human nature and life, and who enjoy that type of intellectual stimulation. 		<username>The Ebullient One</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Thoughtful and spooky drama</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*******Mondo Spoiler Warning***********It's wrong really to classify this movie as horror, since it's really more a study of one man's voyage into darkness. The pace is slow, and thankfully for once the camerawork is also, gving you time to absorb and think about things. This is good as several events in the movie aren't really clear until some time after they happened.Sy the Photo Guy is someone we've all met before, the lonely loser who doesn't seem to have friends or family, and embraces something to hold onto their fleeting life. In Sy's case it's photos, and he's the best darn photo processor you'll meet, even if it's at a second run discount store. Like a maestro spinning music Sy churns out our family pictures, seeing other people's lives and happiness, and knowing how empty he is. Nothing brings that part home more than a scene of the Yorkins's son telling his mother how he feels sorry for Sy and they both try to send him happy thoughts, and at the very moment those thoughts are being sent we see Sy staring off in his kitchen in a look of one seeing the abyss.Anyone who's worked in a department store knows how humiliating it is to put on the little vest and work a shift of phony smiles, and Sy on the surface is the model employee. He is as the neat and tidy as the store aisles around him, but beneath it, though, his soul is quite literally missing. He is ever yearning for something more. Sy is so into the background it's almost as if he was one of the store mannequins had come to life. This moment is portrayed ever so clearly when Sy learns he is losing his job, and sits dejectedly on what could have been his bed, until the camera pulls back and we see they're floor models in the furniture section, and Sy is transparent to the shoppers around him. It's never explained why he chose the Yorkins as his family to dream of, but he lives vicariously through them. It would have been easy and a little cheap to make Sy's desires focus on the wife or even worse the child, but instead he wants to be Uncle Sy, the fourth member of the family. The film stumbles a little on only a few plot points, for example I wonder how Sy could be making extra photos for himself for literally years and only recently his boss clued into it, and why Sy didn't just ring the photos up for cash out of his pocket when no one was looking, that way they never would have figured it out.Much could and likely will be made of the ending, my interpretation is thus: Sy makes a one line statement at the end about children being forced to do horrible things in pictures. Sy was probably abused as a child and photographed while it happened, which explains his life long fascination with photos and how they're supposed to capture happiness. As Sy's world crumbles after losing his job he realizes Will Yorkin was having an affair, and considers that as much the cause as the symptom of his troubles. Sy gets his revenge the way he's lived his life, by photos. He makes his boss think he's going to harm his daughter, and forces Will and Maya to be humiliated just like he had been, the worst punishment in his world that can be meted out.Having Sy actually take pictures of everything in the room but the couple is the real thinking point. I've heard one theory that Sy was never even in the room and fantasizing the whole thing, taking photos of what he imagined he was doing. That's plausible, but considering how Will and Maya are acting when the cop arrives I don't think so. My feeling is the camera Sy was using on Will and Maya was actually empty of film, and I think it's more Sy wanted to teach them a lesson but have it be something that would be forgotten, to deny them even the photos of what they were going through. He instead took pictures of everything in the room he was staying in down the hall. Philosophically it was showing Sy's place in things, that no matter what he does he's always what's overlooked, the things people don't see.The final shot of Sy with the family is to me his vain desire to retreat into a dreamworld, even though he's rooted here in the cold and empty shell of reality.Overall a very good movie, and definitely one worth thinking about.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Cipher-J</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Making the most from mental illness.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A reclusive, dissociated neurotic, whose personal reality is going through a projected identification with a fantasy world, created around a family whose photos he has been developing for years, has rapidly progressed from disturbed thinking to the obsessive, and needs only a push to go over the edge to a full blown psychosis. If he had been showing up at work with, say, a chronic cough, or some other "physical" ailment, his manager would have required that he see his doctor about it. Similarly, an intelligent manager, recognizing his mental irregularities as symptomatic of a psychological condition, could have saved the day by requiring that he become involved in professional counseling as a condition of continued employment. But the manager is a fool, so he just pushes the guy right over the edge.Typical reviews of this film tend to focus only on the most obvious and superficial: That it succeeds as a well crafted psychological thriller; that hard though it may be to get the image of "Mrs. Doubtfire" out of the way long enough to imagine Robin Williams as a mentally disturbed individual, spiraling out of control into desperation, nevertheless he gives a convincing performance, and so forth. Unfortunately, while it might be highly entertaining as a thriller, it does a serious disservice to issues related to mental illness. If the manager had acted responsibly, there would have been no basis for the plot--that much is granted. But all too many managers in the "real" world act with similar disregard in matters related to human dignity, and that is a point sadly underplayed in this story. Instead, the finger is only pointed at the "victim" of the manager's callousness, the lonely creepy "crazy" guy.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It's all about the cinematography</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I thought this movie was going to be all about Robin Williams.....trying to change his image. Instead, I have a new favorite DP...Jeff Cronenweth. The cinematography in this movie is remarkable. For me, it stole the show. He was able to mix a surreal, almost Kubrick-esquq style (to show the mental state of the character) with a subtle, almost Dogma style (to mimic the snapshots). It was amazing. He was the DP on Fight Club also. I remember the work on that was good, but I'll have to go back and compare. If you liked Fight Club's camera work, I recommend 'One Hour Photo' I have no doubt that most people will watch this movie and never notice the camera work, but for those who notice, watch this film. The plot is descent, though a bit predictable as it unfolds. The acting is superb. The direction is quite remarkable. Especially considering Mark Romanek is still relatively new to film (I believe this is only his second feature). If you are a film buff, don't overlook this movie simply because of it's mass appeal. If you are not, don't skip it due to it's poor box office sales. It's one of last year's best.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Little negative to say about the film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Playing against type must present a great attraction to any mainstream star bored with making the same film for the tenth time to satisfy their agents demands to have a better defined public persona. Similarly doing a low budget independent film to prove that fame hasn't changed them has flattered more than one ego over the years. Unfortunately the product of these two things is usually the ugly bug a windscreen type mess of an interesting squashed beneath the shortcomings of a showboating star performance. One Hour Photo is the opposite of this.Robin Williams plays Sy, the manager of a the one hour photo development booth at an American supermarket. Sy is courteous, talented and caring about his job, he is also dangerously obsessed with the lives of a family who develop all their pictures at his booth. Soon this obsession is spiralling out of control.To say that Robin Williams is a revelation is an understatement. In fact attempting to adequately enthuse about the subtly restraint and power of a performance that could unsettle a major land mass is likely to cause any reviewer sprained muscles. Suffice to say that Insomnia clearly represented Williams on autopilot and Patch Adams must have been him sleepwalking.The pacing and composition of the film are expertly judged with the ultra normality of the surroundings and the proliferation of whites and greys perfectly capturing the emptiness of the life drawing Sy to insanity. One Hour Photo is never graphic or obscene, but on an emotional level it is devastating and has one of the graceful and inventive endings in years, one that is characteristic of the whole film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-01-09</date>
		<username>(daveyh25@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A Wonderul Motion "Picture"!!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>ONE HOUR PHOTO September 19, 2002 Sy Parrish: Robin Williams Nina Yorkin: Connie Nielsen Will Yorkin: Michael Vartan Bill Owens: Gary Cole Jake Yorkin: Dylan Smith Det. Van Der Zee: Eriq LaSalle Maya Burson: Erin Daniels Fox Searchlight Pictures presents a film written and directed by Mark Romanek. Running time: 98 minutes. Rated R (for sexual content and language).*** Warning: Some Spoilers ***'One-Hour Photo' is a scary movie. No, it's not your typical scary movie that we are accustomed to seeing on the big screen. It's scary because the character that we see on film is not so different than most of us. However, he's not quite right . just a little off, so to speak. He's like a car we drive which has a cracked windshield. The car still runs fine, but it's not the same, and the vision of the world we see through that glass . is just a bit warped at times. Robin Williams plays that character, and his name is Sy Parrish.Sy Parrish has worked in the photo shop at the local SavMart, which is your typical discount store. He loves his job and takes pride in developing the pictures for SavMart's customers. They are not just SavMart's customers . they are HIS customers.One particular customer is a family by the name of Yorkin. Sy pays particular attention to this family, for he keeps a set of prints of them just for himself. He imagines himself being a part of that family. To him, he is `Uncle' Sy. Sy doesn't appear to have any family or friends of his own . so he pretends to have a life that is more meaningful . more significant. To most people, he is just another insignificant person we see in our daily lives. Sy is someone that wants to be accepted and more importantly . loved. He lives in a fantasy world. Why is he this way? What has made him a little more 'cracked' than the rest of us? We don't really have time to think about that as we watch this movie. And, come to think of it, we don't take the time to think about people like Sy in our own lives either.This movie, superbly directed by Mark Romanek, utilizes the camera in ways that I've not seen before in a motion picture. In other words, although this is a `motion' picture, the director lets us take a `snapshot' of the world much how our own mind takes in the series of events we see every day.Robin Williams, in perhaps his best role on screen, is not playing his typical role. Normally, we either see him as a bigger than life character or the typical `Clown'. In this movie, he is neither of these things. He is a lonely man, a man without a sense of purpose, at least not in the real world. He is not very funny, but in fact, quite sad. And, there is nothing more melancholy and gloomy than that of a sad clown.I highly recommend this movie and give it a score of 8.5 on a scale of 1 to 10.- David Hardin</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A change of pace that reminds audiences of Williams' gift as actor</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Synopsis: Lonely photo developer Sy Parrish has spent his entire life in a quiet, mundane existence. With his employment behind the counter of a one hour photo lab being his only solace, there isn't much joy that Sy seeks out. But his one curiosity transforms into an unhealthy obsession with the Yorkin family, a seemingly idyllic suburban family that has it all. That obsession begins to take over Sy's perception of what is real and what isn't.The Review: With this film having sealed up what critics call his "Dark Trilogy", Robin Williams finally puts an end to that perception of critics saying he does roles that are too goody goody. Following the mean spirited "Death To Smoochy" and dreadful atmosphere of "Insomnia", "One Hour Photo" is a performance in which we sympathize with Williams' performance as an outsider of society, damaged by his psychological background and left to wander aimlessly without a family. It's an about face performance for the man who gave us "Patch Adams" and "Dead Poets Society" that is one of his best. The film itself is truly disturbing by undermining a normal, everyday thing such as photo developing, as being something more devious than expected. Mark Romanek's film is intentionally disturbing in the fact that it came from his own mind, with it's dreary atmosphere and odd music. But it's the work of a master who doesn't compromise story in favor of commercialism. Speaking of which, "One Hour Photo" is an art film that's away from the furthest recesses of anything Robin Williams has done in the past five or so years.Grade: B</fullReview>

		<username>PlanecrazyIkarus</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Impressive, clever, with minor flaws.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The story: A family regularly develop their photos at the local Sav-Mart. There, "Sy the photo-guy" takes special care of all their photos. However, he is obsessed with the family, obsessed with the lives that he can only watch from outside, yet feels more a part of than his own. As the movie develops, it gets darker and darker in tone, and giving away any more of the story would be spoiling it.Now, Robin Williams as psycho is a bit of an oddity. In Insomnia, he played another psycho part with a twist of humour, giving the film wit and a dash of dark comedy. Here, he plays the role absolutely straight and sincere. And he succeeds, phenomenally. We know that this isn't Hannibal Lecter, or some serial killer - we seem his as a person, perceive many of his insecurities, his feelings, his obsession. We understand him, and in my case, identify with him.But does that make for good entertainment? In the beginning, the film lags a bit. After a promising start (I expected the movie to be slightly clever but end with a climactic slaughtering of the psychopath in self-defence by the wife - as most average Hollywood movies would have done. Well, the start of the movie makes it quite clear that this outcome is not an option, and so I was delighted) the movie hits a bump of boredom or two. Perhaps that is intentional - we are getting a glimpse of Sy's life and job, which is boredom, but it does not drive the story very much. Also, his intrusions on the family happen too quickly - at the start of the story, he has already obsessed about them for years, so it is harder to understand him than if we had been led there more gradually. But as the plot develops, and takes a twist and turn or two, the pace increases rapidly and towards the end the movie is very thrilling, disturbing, yet brilliant.Of course, there are some flaws. One dream sequence in particular is meant to cause cheap shocks - a boo-scare moment that should have been beneath this movie and feels totally out of place. The boring moments near the start can disappoint a bit. And the child acting is a bit, well, not Haley Joel Osmond...But, despite its flaws, the movie is clever, it misleads the audience several times, it is thrilling and disturbing, and generally very entertaining. Probably one of the best movies this year - and certainly quite original.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>View this movie as if it were a piece of art...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Of all the films I've seen at the theater this year, One Hour Photo has been one of the finest. Its production has taken years, giving Mark Romanek plenty of time to tweak his magnum opus to a searing perfection. There are little clues of foreshadowing, that you'll miss, if you don't pay close attention to the film. Robin William's acting is so vivid and convincing that it's hard to believe he once portrayed the flamboyant Mrs. Doubtfire. And the excellent use of colors, lighting and editing to convey emotions, is almost identical to the masterful cinematography of American Beauty.I must admit I wasn't thrilled about seeing a movie about an obsessive stalker, but to enjoy and appreciate this film, one doesn't have to like its subject matter. When my mother asked me if she would like it, I said, `View this movie as if it were a piece of art.' If you like a painting, you're going to like how it looks and makes you feel. If you judge One Hour Photo based upon its disturbing content, you haven't fully appreciated its utter brilliance.</fullReview>

A terse and almost uncomplicated storyline are what's in store,both in the white and superstored environment of the protagonist and also a reason for the imagery of his thinking.Seymore's world is very neat &amp; tidy ,but also wide open and exposed,not terribly unlike his job. To take film development orders and complete the exposures to their completion,hopefull by day's end.What transpires in-between those hrs. waiting for film to develop and customer to return for pickup are what fill his world and also the tragic void,within his dreary and lovelorn life. As the movie progresses from simple and pleasant photo man to ill-tempered kook,on a mission to find that which has abandoned and sheltered his sense of right and wrong. Seymore starts out being a nice guy and overly competent and polite, but turns into a monster,befitting a psycho movie sans weird castle or haunted house. But nevertheless,Seymore gives a contemporary view into madness and all it's weird pontification. I give it a 7/10 for lackluster finish but a wielding approach.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-11-13</date>
		<username>Aidan McGuinness</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>`Snap' up this film with Robin Williams in great acting shock!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Well this movie proves one thing - `Insomnia' was not a one-off for Robin Williams. He's capable of doing dark roles and doing them very well. In `One Hour Photo' he plays Seymour Parrish, a photo developer, who becomes obsessed with pictures, and the family, of one his clients. How far will this obsession go?Well I won't spend an age talking about the plot. There's not too much there - more a gradual decline by Seymour as his obsession with the Yorkins grows. There's an element of Yorkin being crazy - having visions. The photos of course act as a tool - showing how Seymour can only relate to people from a distance, contemplating from afar. There's a great sadness in them. Much of this is helped by an excellent performance by Robin Williams. He carries Seymour with a great sadness in him - in his eyes, his posture, and the lines on his face. Even when angry, you can see Williams is showing that the anger is directed not so much at the person in front of him, but at the ghosts of his past. This movie is essentially about Williams and that's one of the problems. No one else really gets a look in. Sure we see the Yorkins but we mostly only see them through Seymour. We don't ever really get to know them and so we cannot empathise with them as Seymour's obsession grows. It's a flaw but it's not a killing blow.Mark Romanek, who wrote and directed, shows some nice skill with the set-up of scenes. Seymour is decked out in light colours - typically cream. Dressed blandly and inoffensively, he blends into the dull workplace and home. He doesn't want to be seen. He also allows a bit of humour into the movie, with Seymour's wonderful discourse about his customers' photo habits. It not only alleviates the tension in a generally downbeat movie, but allows Seymour to become than the brooding-weirdo cliché. The movie is structured in a relatively conventional scene, recounted in flashback form with Seymour at the police station. It allows the audience to wonder how he got there - and is it for the reasons we think. The final few minutes are very satisfying as a result.`One Hour Photo' is very character based and succeeds because of (and not in spite of) Robin Williams' performance. You cannot help but feel sympathy for this man and watch with sadness as his tale unfolds. Shot well, scripted with enough care that you are left bored, it's a movie that - while not maybe worthy of many viewings - is certainly worth seeing. 7.5/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

Well lets start with start with Robin Williams character Sy. Robin Williams plays a photo developer who becomes obsessed with the family of a long time customer. Williams is a lonely man who entire life revolves around the photo mat and store it resides in. Williams does a great job playing the underdeveloped character to the best of his ability. I never understood why he acted the way he did until the vary end. Where he, instead of though dialogue and acting, openly bares all to the arresting officer, played by Eriq La Salle from E.R. The idea behind it all was a good one, a man, who works in a photo developing lab, sees the world through the pictures taken by other people. And since most pictures are happy ones i.e., birthdays, graduations, vacations. This is how he develops an obsession for a fairy tale family, a family that he may not have had, although that is purely a guess since the movie never brought out that detail. I would have liked to see the family in question developed much more. In stead we have an extremely sensitive 7 year-old, a husband who is a good provider but not a father, and a beautiful wife who is hung up on her not so perfect husband. I am a person who feels the ending is one of the most critical parts of a good thriller/mystery movie. And let me say, I was extremely disappointed. At no point was I shocked, surprised, stunned, or bewildered. Disturbed? Yes. All in all, Williams delivers the best performance he could have with the material given to him. Though there was little substance it was a good idea, but maybe One Hour Photo could have been one hour longer to develop the characters a little more. I give this movie a C+ generously. You can thank Robin Williams for saving it from being a total disaster. Williams gives us another Oscar worthy performance, but may not get the nod due to the lack of script.One thing I did get from this movie is that I should consider switching to a digital camera.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-10-15</date>
		<username>Mike Weston (mweston)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>3.5 stars (out of 4)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For people who like their thrillers to be thrilling, this film makes a crucial mistake in the very beginning, by telling us more or less what the outcome is. Specifically, we see Seymour ("Sy") Parrish (Robin Williams) in an amazingly sterile, bright white police facility having his picture taken and being questioned by a detective (Eriq La Salle from "ER"). The film is told in flashback.Sy has been working for years as the photo developer at SavMart, which looks a lot like WalMart only brighter and less colorful, like the sterile police station seen earlier. We find out early that he has an interest in the Yorkin family - Nina (Connie Nielson from "Gladiator"), Will, and son Jakob - even memorizing their home address and giving Jakob a disposable camera for his birthday. Creepier still, he gets an extra set of their photos for himself.I won't go into any more detail on the story, to save the remaining surprises.The performance by Robin Williams is remarkable. Despite being very well known and familiar to the audience, he disappears into this character, leaving no trace of the stand-up comedian or "Mrs. Doubtfire." He is bland and deliberate, but with turmoil sensed beneath the surface. This performance should be remembered at Oscar nomination time.But what really made me enjoy the film as much as I did was a feeling that it just *worked.* I don't really know how to describe this feeling I sometimes get (not often enough), except that the films that evoke it often win awards for screen writing. The story is simply told well.But is it a thriller? It has been sold as one, and there are certainly many tense moments, but I think it is really more of a character study of someone you would normally hardly notice. When someone suddenly erupts in violence, going "postal," the neighbors are very often heard to say "he was a quiet man." Sy Parrish was a quiet man.Seen on 10/10/2002.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-10-14</date>
		<username>RainKing-2</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Where's the payoff?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You know, this really seemed like a movie I should like. Seeing the previews and the advance buzz, watching the movie, even thinking about it and talking with Amanda about it afterwards, I thought, "Damn, that was a solid piece of film making. Then why didn't I like it?"And three days later, I'm still not sure if I have an answer.One Hour Photo is the story of Sy the Photo Guy, a negative pusher at Sav-Mart. He takes a nearly sexual satisfaction in his work. He's personable, careful, one of those guys that you enjoy talking to for the 45 seconds you're dropping off your prints, and instantly forget about.At the opening of the film, you know that he's in jail for something. You don't know what, and you're kind of scared to find out. He's so meticulous, you know that he could be pure, delicious evil. It's uncovered that he has a slight obsession with the Yorkins, a family that's brought their prints to him for years. He's watched their little boy grow up, one 4x6 at a time. And he identifies a little too much with them.Robin Williams is perfect for the role, for the same reason he was perfect for the role in Insomnia, another film I didn't like. He's so reserved, so particular, that you identify very strongly with his character. He's just a lonely guy that wants a family of his own. He just doesn't know quite how to go about it in a healthy way.This is the first film from music-video director Mark Romanek. His videos have always had a certain care to them, an attention to detail. Fiona Apple's "Criminal", NIN's "Closer", Lenny Kravitz' "Are You Gonna Go My Way" have all been examples for the genre - showy, attention-grabbing, information-packed. He has the same attention for detail in One Hour Photo. Tom Foden, the production designer for Tarsem's The Cell, shows the same eye for color and specific detail. The design of Sav-Mart is perfect. It's the soulless, impersonal discount store that we've all been to, complete with the manager's one-way window over the aisles, except it's been whitewashed and sterilized even more. The aisle ends have the contents listed in a tiny, tiny font that makes you feel insignificant.The fact that the photo booth is so far away from the rest of the store is fairly significant. He's dealing with the happiest memories of people lives that have been captured onto little bits of plastic. People speak to him for moments at a time, and then resume their lives as he handles what was. He's just that guy, shoved off in the corner, an afterthought.So why didn't I like One Hour Photo? To figure it out, I had to look at other films that were psychologically disturbing that I did enjoy: Aranofsky's Requiem for a Dream and Fincher's Seven. Both films leave you feeling grimy, unclean, like you need a shower in sulfuric acid or something. But with both of those films, there was a big payoff or something that made you feel like it was worth the trip. In Seven, there's the big surprise ending that makes you reconsider everything that had come before. In Requiem, there's the overarching message about addiction and its effect on the human spirit.In One Hour Photo, you don't have that. You've simply got a guy that, while he's extremely well-drawn, is a little crazy. You don't have the overall moral. You don't have the big payoff. (There's almost a reverse payoff. You'll see what I mean.)In One Hour Photo, you've simply got a guy who wants a family, loves cameras, and is a little nuts. And for me, that wasn't worth the trip.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Andrew Hutchings</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The strange Parrish clan...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So, this is the user review site but part of me wants to ask a trivia question - namely, which other actor, if any, has ever played two separate characters with the same surname? Robin Williams has managed it. Several years ago he played Alan Parrish, who got himself trapped in the jungle for 26 years, courtesy of a strange board game. Now he's Sy Parrish, who has obviously been trapped in the jungle of his mind for all of his life. I am convinced the Parrish clan contains a lot of weird and wonderful characters...but Sy appears to have no relatives at all. He exists only to develop family snapshots to the best of his ability, and of those families, one captures his attention so much that he begins to fel actually a part of their lives. This is not a movie for crash, bang, wallop merchants. It's got something called tension; something which is lacking in many thrillers, forfeited for cheap gory interludes. I am not normally a big Robin Williams fan. He's made some bad movies, and was not too convincing in 'Insomnia' playing the villain. But here, he comes of age in a role in which Anthony Hopkins would apparently have been more suited. But Williams deserves rich acclaim. There are one or two moments when the character appears ominously real. In fact, this is Oscar-material, from his point of view. The colourless images on screen, especially of Williams' scenes, are perfect to suit the mood of the piece. I do feel the 'flashback' tool (if you'll pardon the pun) is a mistake in some ways, but it doesn't alter the powerful message. Obsessions can be very dangerous. Anyone who has had one will appreciate this fact. I have had; and I do, respectively. This isn't going to sweep the board on Oscars night, but it is a refreshing, if morose, change of style. I've heard comparisons with Hitchcock (again!) and in this case they are quite justified. Not a film you will ever forget, especially when you next get a roll of film developed! 7/10.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Watching this film made me feel one sensation only. Cold. very VERY cold. But that's not necessarily a bad thing. In his lonelyness he ends up fantasizing about belonging to a real family that frequents the store to drop off film. Sy's store front is awash in a see of white, bland looking and hopefuly empty. Here he works and not so much as an employee but more of a fixture to an already lifeless world. Much of the cinematography is awash in a see of white, from an interogation room that Sy is held in flashbacks, and the store front of never ending white shelves. The film also has a grainy touch creating an even more sterile feel into Sy's world.Nevertheless, the film is a little slow plodding, and it's a gradual but sure climactic climb. Also one note of interest is that Sy's boss is played by Gary Cole, who was also the annoying boss in "Office Space". About those TPS reports..." The hard thing is believing that this guy use to be an A+ class comic clown. Seems Robin can inherit the world of serial killers like nobodys business. The dilemma is whether to give him a best actor nomination for either this film or Insomnia.See this film for Robin Williams performance alone.Rating 7.5 out of 10</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2002-09-29</date>
		<username>JasonJayDelmonico</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Thought provoking and sad, despite Romanek's overblown direction</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Final Score (calculated average of various classic cinematic qualities):8.2 (out of 10)People often ask why do we need critics and why do we need award shows other than to have all the snotty Hollywood elitist peers pat themselves on the back. Robin Williams gives a performance in this movie that is not only his best to date, it occurs to me that this is why we give out Oscars for acting in the first place. This is a guy that in this film - away from his usual mugging, sappy roles and jokes about Prozac - really embodies what the craft of acting is all about. He immerses himself so deeply into the character of Sy Parrish that it might actually come as a shock to see the name "Robin Williams" over the end credits. When a director does a good job he is rewarded with another job on a promising script, when a studio markets a movie well they are rewarded with a big opening weekend at the box office and when the movie itself is good it is rewarded at the video counter when you buy it and take it home for your library. When an actor does a job as well as Williams does with this movie the least you can do is give him a trophy to put on his mantel. Every once in a while a movie is worth seeing simply because the lead performance drives it to being good where it otherwise might not have been. Dustin Hoffman did it with "Rain Man", Dustin Hoffman did it with "Wag the Dog" and Robin Williams does it with "One Hour Photo".The movie itself is a great idea. How much does the guy that processes your photos know about you? Or more importantly what does the sum of the pictures you've taken say about your life? Your family? and the life you wish you had? The script, in Sy's musing over why photos are so important and what they say about us, is a real thought provoker. The story unfolds as a real puzzler with the operative question hanging over our heads the whole time "How far will Sy go?". But to me "One Hour Photo" isn't just about making you think twice about where you take your pictures. It is to make you think twice about everybody. By focusing the film on a quiet, lonely man that we otherwise wouldn't give a second thought scribe Mark Romanek makes a comment about the system we all live in. We don't really think the guy processing your photos or busing your table or cutting your hair has a purpose in life or has needs other than to serve us at that moment. In many ways the film is like "Psycho", which does not necessarily mean it is a rip-off. The thriller elements in the 3rd act are fully realized and yet true to the characters. I don't believe Sy is evil or deranged, just delusional and misguided. He seems to genuinely love the Yorkins. But it shows how love, in the wrong mind, can easily slip into obsession. His efforts to fit into society (like reading the books they do) are realistic, clumsy, sweet and at times genuinely heart-breaking. Finally, the look of the movie is top-notch with vibrant colors. However, it is hampered by a couple of things. Number one being the music. The music itself is great - particularly the creepy chimes as Sy walks down the hotel hall in the finale - it just seems like it's in the wrong movie. In fact, Romanek's entire style seems off. He doesn't seem to realize that this is a subtle, quiet independent film taking us into the mind of a quiet man. As a result he blasts the speakers out with a bunch of false music cues and frenzied camera work. It reminded me of Mark Pellington's style in "Arlington Road" and while it worked for that film it doesn't here. Granted he does create intensity in the 3rd act, but the rest of the film seem completely overblown. The opening shot of the camera is built up like it's going to release armageddon. Not to mention the scene where the Yorkin's send good thoughts to Sy and Romanek cues up the music to a frenzied pace as if to suggest Sy is somehow telepathically receiving their thoughts. It's laughably bad. All of this contradicts the more subtle distant stuff. One of the best moments in the film is the way Romanek shows- by not showing- Mrs. Yorkin's reaction to her husband's infidelity as Sy watches from a following car. Ultimately, despite the director's efforts to make a different movie, I think "One Hour Photo" works as a portrait of a sad man, as an examination of photography, as a visual stunner, as a puzzler without clear heroes or villains and as a well acted piece. In all the fuss about Williams it also should be mentioned that Connie Nielson holds her own in a fantastic 2nd bill, but lead performance. As does Gary Cole doing a complete 180 on his cartoonish "Office Space" boss. </fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Do not miss One Hour Photo if you like to predict Oscar Winners</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour PhotoIf you enjoy film, do not miss One Hour Photo, a psychological thriller.Robin Williams gives an Oscar worthy performance as Sy, the photo guy at a local Savmart. Robin Williams is unrecognizable as Sy. He is a sad, disturbed, lonely character who creates a fantasy world, in which he is a part of a young suburban family. Sy creates this connection to the family because he has been developing their family pictures for almost a decade. Eventually, his obsession with the family turns psychotic. Sy intervenes in the family dynamics using particularly twisted methods. Robin Williams does a remarkable job of making the viewer empathize with his craziness.One Hour Photo is written and directed by Mark Romanek. He captures the lonely, desperate world of Sy brilliantly. One technique he uses is emphasizing Sy's squeaky work shoes as he walks down the aisles of Savmart. The viewer feels his desperation, isolation, and loneliness. Romanek deserves an Oscar nomination for his clever script, which grips the audience in the first scene and holds their attention.One Hour Photo is not for the weak. It is dark and depressing. If you plan on predicting the Oscar winners then this is a must see.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>One Hour Photo is not exactly an enjoyable movie, but a very well made and interesting one where Robin Williams plays a lonely and quite possibly psychotic photo guy working at a place surreally like WalMart who seems a bit too infatuated with a young family whose photos he has been developing for years. 		<shortDescription>I Figure This Is Probably Mel Gibson's Bad Dream...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(POSSIBLE SPOILER)This morbid little quasi-thriller boasts impressive direction and good performances, but it's part of a movie trend which is even more alarming than the use of KING OF KINGS-style Heavenly choirs in the musical scores of pedestrian crime dramas and silly action/adventure romps... Call it genre-tweaking, or Dead Greek Male-bashing, it should be against the law of Movieland to present without the slightest differentiation a sequence of events which are only vaguely implausible (not like riding a tornado from a black-and-white Dust Bowl to a Technicolor fairyland) and then have them suddenly turn out to be someone's daydream... To crash the relationships of the main characters into a brick wall with about forty minutes of show to go. As for that weisenheimer sicko/ gotcha! ending, it's as cheap a trick as a greeting card which promises something naughty on the cover and inside has only a lame joke meant to shame the reader... But back to Aristotle. The opposite of catharsis is not a pretty thing...It's a shame. If the writer/director had played it straighter, had he not been so set on engaging in postmodern antics with the psychokiller thriller genre, this easily could have made for some great human comedy/drama. I'd bet that a good script doctor would have ordered that Sy try somehow to SAVE the Yorkin's sad situation. That might have led to something genuine, quirky, and interesting- embarrassing, disturbing, and touching all at once. But that would have cramped the trailer's style... So... We get instead a replay the usual psychokiller cliches- ingenious preliminaries to mayhem of which Hannibal Lecter could be proud..The good points... Everyone praises the direction. The last shots, of course, are a rip-off from PSYCHO, but I was thinking not so much of Hitchcock or David Lynch as of Peter Weir for most of the movie... Robin Williams is especially good when he gives the objects of his obsession the brush-off, his angry, despairing rebuff as out-of-whack as his warm devotion had been. (I just hope that our ever sadder clown is not revving up for a John Wayne Gacy biopic...) As the late Gene Siskel used to say, "I wanted to see more" of that oddball family: the all-time wimp father who is as hard as Sy to look at by the end, the kindly but clinically depressed little boy, the speciously perky and chic Mom crying out for help with her defiantly Bad Hair... Above all, I think, the always memorable Gary Cole as a middle management type, one who's too uptight and insensitive to qualify as a Regular Joe, but not quite mean or unreasonable enough to qualify as an..., well, what middle management types are so often called... </fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Beautiful craft--ultimate point?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a beautifully crafted film -- a piece of art, where every frame, every choice is true artistry. I have thought a lot about this film, and I respect the filmmakers and actors and artists who contributed to it -- like Kubrick's THE SHINING in its use of music, for instance -- and yet, ultimately, I am left wondering why this high level of talent was used for this film with its ultimate lack of anything that we take from it. Is this just another look at an American psycho? Do we really need to see another person go crazy? Don't we see enough of that in real life, in real news -- need we make up more of these stories about people who terrorize others. No matter the reason, or the ending differing from other typical Hlywd stories -- it's still the same thing we're being sold -- about a man who goes off his rocker and terrorizes other people -- and, ultimately, you want to ask the artists involved: why? There is nothing redemptive here, no room for change, or hope. Just a sick and hurt person, who has injured others in his pain. Do we really want to make this one of our storytelling highpoints in film? Such beauty, such craft, such talent -- for what?</fullReview>
