		<username>Adam Bernstein (zensixties)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Best Actor Oscar Robin Williams/Best Screenplay Mark Romanek</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo is excellent from beginning to end. Of course there is much more to it then meets the eye. Robin's latest non-comedic role is that of Sy the Camera Guy, a friendless, familyless man who's childhood demons are about to catch up with him.  He lives vicariously through the Yorkin's, a seemingly ideal family who he develops film for. Robin's narrative IS significant, as it gives many cryptic clues as to what the film's really about. (SPOILERS--SPOILERS--SPOILERS) Sy collects extra sets of the Yorkin photos and puts them on his wall, seeing Jake's childhood as the ideal one he wished he had. The background music by Reinhold Heil and Johnny Klimek (Run Lola Run) sets the melancholy mood of despair as we are given clue after clue as to his actual abused childhood, starting with the red eye effect in an actual photo of him and family. Then the Deepak Chopra quote, "What we fear most, already happend to us." Then his telling Jake he broke his collarbone as a kid by "falling from a tree". The lid on his demons explodes when he develops photos showing Will's infidelity, then gets fired by a fascist boss.  Then the elaborate plan goes into effect. He threatens his boss, leaving clues for the authorities, and finally in cathartic release acts out his own abuse, his abusive father taking him over. Then the regression to the ideal childhood, hence the photos of objects a la Jake's photos. Importantly we don't know all this until the end, when he explains it to the detective. Of course this will go over the heads of many in the mainstream audience, so we get the "weak ending" criticisms that show they don't get it. This is a subjective jump into Sy's tortured world and the most appropriate comparison would be to Midnight Cowboy. The parellel to Joe Buck's isolation and naivetee, his disconnection with the TV and radio shows he watches/listens to for example is strikingly similar.This film works on every level and if any justice exists will sweep the Oscars.</fullReview>

		<username>Paul Creeden</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Lacks mature humanity.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robin Williams delivers a brilliant performance. The art direction is wonderful. The cinematography is also great. But, the film presents the same shallow stereotypes about human behavior as a cheapo Halloween film might do with more camp and gore. Single poor middle aged men are scary or bad or crazy or gay or all of the above. Married pretty young people have everything and feelings too. All single poor middle aged men must therefore hate or envy all young pretty married people. These cultural stereotypes are the basis of the film's credibility. They are derived from the sensational press stories of psycho serial killers and such. The film would make excellent propaganda against anyone who deviates from the nuclear family norm. The hotel scene seems especially confused to me. One minute, the Robin Williams character seems like Dirty Harry, and then he seems like Hannibal Lechter. The thematic core seems adolescent. 		<date>2002-09-18</date>
		<username>superjxiv</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Thoughtful, intelligent puzzle</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Though you may not of heard of Mark Romanek, the writer/director of "One Hour Photo," you've probably seen a few of his music videos. One of his most famous is the video for "Closer" by Nine Inch Nails. Knowing this, would you be surprised to know that "One Hour Photo" is, in a nutshell . . . creepy?Evoking a visual style reminiscent of Stanley Kubrick, Romanek's engrossing story tells the tale of the lonely photo clerk Sy Parrish. He obsesses over the smallest details of his job as though each photo was a work of art, and he is as proud of the pictures he develops as those who took them. Robin Williams' performance as the idiosyncratic and solitary Sy is breath-taking.The plot of the movie isn't as important as its style. Sy spends about half the movie fantasizing of a better life. We are guiding very carefully through some of these dreams, both waking and unconscious. Other dreams we enter as they occur, and Sy's mind is our only guild. We are never too sure what is really happening and what is all in Sy's head.Sy wants nothing more than a family that loves him, and if he had to choose, it'd be the Yorkins. Will, Nina and little Jakob, they are the, ahem, picture of suburban happiness. Sy should know; every time they get a role of film developed, Sy keeps a copy for himself.But all is not well in the Yorkin house. There are fights over money, negligence, and other things that never make it to the one hour photo. When Sy discovers his ideal family is being torn apart, the movie gets a little .. . weird. Or should I say, weirder. This is where a typical movie would turn Sy into a mass murderer or some kind of maniacal ranting loony. Instead, Sy chooses his actions and his words with more care. He hones his attention to detail like a conductor of a symphony and tracks down the people that are responsible for shattering his dream world. His ultimate revenge is probably the hardest to understand. This is where the movie becomes the most difficult to watch, but where the most intrigue lays. To understand his actions in this scene, and indeed throughout the movie, we must try to understand Sy's entire life. Romanek's skill as a director is that he gives us the tools to decode Sy's odder behavior, but doesn't spell it out in obvious terms. While it's not the greatest film in its genre, its certainly worth a look to anyone interested in some intense character study. And as a foot note, I can't tell you how great it was to see the second movie in as many weeks that featured bleeding eyes. Ug. I need a shower.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Predictable</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Usually thrillers like this are shown on minor television stations at about ten p.m. and never find their way to the movies (only, of course, if someone like Robin Williams stars). I was invited to a preview of this film, and I got negatively surprised. The plot of One Hour Photo is based on the idea that it is quite spooky to think of somebody who knows a lot about you because he regularly develops your photographs. A nice idea this is but that's where it ends: The story is predictable and the dialogues are tense and artificial, therefore no suspense arises, and after a couple of minutes it's perfectly clear how things are going to develop. Robin Williams is brilliant, though, but he has no competitors. The family he persecutes is boring and pale, and Connie Nielsen stands out concerning soap-opera-acting.An obvious means of style is the use of pale colours only to present the main character's every-day surroundings. I am not quite sure whether to regard this as a good trick or just as a lack of creativity. In the end, the main character explains his whole life (and the whole film) to the audience, and it doesn't take him more than two minutes. I guess this is how this movie came into existence: the writers presented their idea to the producers, it didn't take them longer than ten sentences, the producers were convinced, and everything else was just a matter of time and money.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>We're accustomed to Robin Williams playing quirky characters. From the unscripted Mork to the unconventional Patch Adams, we have learned to cut him slack in taking roles to enhance his range rather than his image. This one is a stark contrast to his recent touring performances and HBO special, and perhaps it's his way of achieving balance. We see a remarkable portrayal of Seymour (Sy) Parrish, a lonely guy getting his life experiences by vicarious means and an extra copy of selected prints. Without a life of his own, Sy lives through his fantasies triggered by the photos he expertly develops. His narrative on the lives of select regular patrons of his shop is a classic of middle American profiling. And his strict attention to detail brings him close to blows with a repair technician whose reality is closer to pedestrian standards.Beige is Sy's theme from his clothes and car to most of his apartment. The contrast between his sallow life and his target fantasy family is vivid. His only friends seem to be imaginary as Sy move from fantasy to reality in dream sequences that get more bizarre as the story moves forward.Gary Cole portrays the typical discount store manager (Bill Owens) who's waiting to pounce on any oversight as he peers out from the perch overlooking his domain. His management style, as evidenced by Sy's discharge, is a textbook example of bad management, but more the rule rather than the exception for a mall manager. When Sy begins to use his camera as a weapon (with an interesting explanation of the derivation of the word "snapshot") he really goes for revenge. And by the time he's through, you have a very strong argument for digital photography and marital fidelity.In the absence of a dark room - since these high-tech developers do it in broad daylight - we have a very dark film. Not one that will give you nightmares, but you'll never again leave your real address with the clerk at your one-hour photo. </fullReview>

		<username>jjh6519</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Oscar Nom for Robin?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film was far better than I expected. *** SPOILER ALERT, SORT-OF *** What could have easily, on a number of occasions, turned into a story filled with cliches and formulas seen a dozen times before in movies about psychologically disturbed people, the director-writer chooses instead (to his credit) to make this a study of a character, with actual character development. I expected, so many times, that a certain standard Hollywood plot twist would naturally happen, but it did not. I thank the writer-director for such inventiveness and restraint. Robin Williams, along with the script and direction, takes what would otherwise be a two-dimensional character (Sy Parrish, or "Sy the Photo Guy"), and develops him (pun intended) in various ways. The most important way is to allow the audience see the peels coming off the seemingly rigid outside of an otherwise fragile, insecure, soft-in-the-middle, and pathetic human being. But Williams is so good at this role, that we even are allowed to feel empathy (not sorry, not pity) for this lonely man, this truly disturbed, isolated individual. And we even get to understand him, thanks to the writer!, quite the opposite of what we expect when Hollywood uses a plot about a dangerous person. This is not an evil person -- it is someone who desperately needs to feel loved and needed. Then two events shatter his entire self-made life, and he becomes downright scary.When Sy's isolation and how he deals with it, is depicted throughout, the Simon and Garfunkel song "I Am a Rock" somehow comes to mind. Also, early in the film, when we first see Sy sitting alone watching "The Simpsons" (a cartoon family, just as unreal as the seemingly ideal family he develops in his own mind), and as the camera pulls back, we see the extent of his obsession and madness. But it reminds you of the Robert DeNiro character rehearsing at his home in "The King of Comedy".I think that Robin Williams should expect at least a nomination for best actor. He is superb as someone who is both chilling and frightening and also who needs to be loved and treated with care. His Sy Parrish might end up being as famous as Norman Bates and Travis Bickel.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>My favorite thing about this film was the fact that the lead character Seymour Parrish, played by Williams, was not as black and white (no pun intended) as many of these so called 'psychos' we see in these thrillers popping up all over the place. We understood and are as the film rolls to a close made to understand what has driven this man and how, given his life, we may make the same choices he has made. It's amazing to see this man transform himself from the insane comic to this brilliantly played character. It does nothing but reassure us, the audience, that Mr. Williams is not only one of the greatest actors of our time, but maybe one of the finest ever. This is not to deny the well-played performances of the supporting cast. The ever beautiful and sexy, Connie Nielsen portrays the ever-loving mother, who even in the face of tragedy will never do anything to break up her family. Also, at times it is hard to remember that young Dylan Smith is an actor and not a young boy thrust into this situation. My applause goes out to the cast of this motion picture.However, a film cannot survive on it's cast alone. My hat goes off to director Mark Romanek. A fan of Romanek's work as a music video director (particularly his work with Trent Reznor of Nine Inch Nails) I was simply blown away by his full length picture debut. I was very impressed with the use of the RGB. Anyone who knows any thing about the production of photography knows that the colors are done in levels of Red Green and Blue. The way these three colors are schemed throughout the fill was utterly brilliant. Camera use and cinematography also help capture the surreal and horrifying reality of this film.I was also very found off the soundtrack. Its simplicity gave the film the perfect feel. If you look at the track from start to finish, you'll realize that the entire score is one huge build from start to finish. It really pushes you toward what's coming next, which is what the thriller is all about.Overall I give it a 9 out of 10. And if you know me, nobody ever gets a 10.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>[SPOILER, SPOILER, SPOILER---I am labeling this analysis as a spoiler. It does not actually give the end. But reading will help the viewer enjoy the movie--like seeing the cameo of Hitch in any of his movies.]____________________________________________________________________________ ____________________________________________________________________I read up on this movie at this site yesterday, but went to see it today. NOW I understand why some viewers were disappointed. I DEFINITELY WAS NOT!!! THIS WAS AN OUTSTANDING MOVIE!! The telescoping symbols in OHP tell a consistent, subtextual story that has the viewer 'put the pieces together.' The writer/director, I believe intentionally eliminated outright, 'right-foot,left-foot' lame tell all storytelling, but allowed the viewer to assemble the information cumulatively. This is a rare intelligent psychological thriller--like The Talented Mr. Rippley--only not so dark.Telescoping examples: RW character is exemplified in the shot of the pet hamster, running as fast as he could in the exercise wheel. Hamster lived within the given confines, and responded with reflexive normalcy.Meaning: Sy is a person living the caged and kept 'hamster's life'. He waits for the caretaker to feed him (Sy feeds hamster/Savmore feeds Sy).The cage is Sy's knowlege and practice of confining animal expression wanting to get out, but not knowing how.The wheel is a telescoping metaphore for daily activities of life; whether the hamster goes fast, or slow, he does not go anywhere or exceed the boundaries of the cage.The 'eye' is a symbol for ultimately individual perceptual subjectivity in life. Life is as we see it is.Some examples: photography as life sustaining, medical corneal lecture at hotel, the blood from Sy's eye in his mind, the argument in the store (3+ blue miscalibration)Meaning: a telescoping hint that Sy is highly influenced by the [eye's] information. He built his life around flat visuals--the pictures--and 'all was well' perception. The perception was again put forward when after being fired, Sy turns up a photo on the floor and it is blank. This is as far as the director went in [obviously] putting out the key his symbols for examination.I study movies for symbolic content--and HIGHLY RECOMMEND this one. Robin Williams is an outstanding actor--and proved it here. Anyone else "do metaphors"??</fullReview>

		<date>2002-09-10</date>
		<username>MICHAEL O'FARRELL (mpofarrell)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Photo Positive/Photo Negative</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In ONE HOUR PHOTO Robin Williams gives an electrifying performance as a photo lab technician in a giant retail emporium (generically named SAV-MART in the movie). A slight , bespectacled middle-aged man with nondescript features, Seymour "Sy the Photo Guy" Parish is fastidious in appearance (his blue work smock is always neatly ironed) and meticulous in his dealings with customers ( almost overly polite, he nimbly handles customers' rolls of film as if they were prized possessions). Sy's voice-over narration at the film's beginning stresses his obsession with photographic perfection and gives a strong clue that for Sy, work is everything. Inherent in the job is the role of voyeur, for Sy peruses the countless number of negative turned -to -positive photos as records of the day to day lives of his customers. He hones in particularly on the pictures of one attractive suburban family, the Yorkins, whose negatives he has been developing for years. When pretty mom Nina Yorkin drops off her film for processing, Sy never fails to ask how the family is doing, and charms Nina's young son Jakob with surprise gifts ( a disposable camera). When Nina's husband Will meets Sy for the first time while looking for an item in the store, he is nonplussed by Sy's overly-friendly manner and familiarity with family names and events. In this scene and in others, the audience feels a discomfort that is almost palpable. Sy Parrish is a desperately lonely man ; when not at work he spends his nights in his dingy, second - story apartment sitting on his sofa in a catatonic state, watching episodes of The Simpsons. His living room is spare and barren but for one wall that is covered with photos.......As a character study of a social misfit, ONE HOUR PHOTO relies heavily on Robin Williams' brilliant acting, which covers mightily for what is essentially a scenario that has been played out ad nauseam on countless made for t.v. movies. Mark Romanek, directing his first feature film after years of helming some of the best music videos (featuring David Bowie, Madonna and Nine Inch Nails among others), imbues this movie with an edgy atmosphere that makes it compulsively watchable if not particularly entertaining. His screenplay never really explains why Sy acts the way he does , and a vague reference by Sy toward the film's conclusion of past abuse doesn't dramatically satisfy. The other characters take a back seat to the Williams portrayal; not fully fleshed out, they seem to serve merely as victims to Sy's explosive rage. This film is, however, an impressive first effort by a promising talent, and a must see for anyone wanting to witness Robin Williams giving the performance of his life.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>DALEM32 (DALEM32@MSN.COM)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Clever, Complex, But Not Complete</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sy Parrish (Robin Williams) says, in the show's opening, "No one ever takes a photograph of something they want to forget." This is an important comment to keep in mind throughout the course of the film. After receiving some very troubling news at work, Sy finds a picture lying on the floor. He picks it up only to find it is blank, representing a point in Sy's life that he would obviously like to forget. The movie is both clever and complex, but yet not thoroughly developed. I left the film wanting something more; more background, more insight. We do not know why Sy first selected the Yorkin family as the object of his obsession. Nor do we learn much about Sy's life either. We are only given a few brief bits of sketchy information with which to form our own conclusions. These rather fuzzy details are an interesting contrast to the meticulous way in which Sy routinely develops the Yorkin family photos.The film helps to illustrate Williams' range as an actor. I would say that Sy is a complex character, but not a menacing, or even disturbing one. He did not threaten any member of the Yorkin family until Will Yorkin (Michael Vartan) began to do that himself with his own neglectful and unfaithful actions. The film also shows us that Williams still has the ability to make us laugh regardless of the role he is portraying. Despite the film's incompleteness, I could not help but like it. It is an enjoyable glimpse into human behavior and its consequences. We come in contact with people everyday, some on a regular basis. One Hour Photo is a story of one of them.</fullReview>

The couple seems to be a bit 'shaggy' in my opinion and Gary Cole gave a performance that makes you wonder how he crawled out of the 'TV and straight-to-video' click with Mike Pare and Judith Light. These things did nothing to hurt the movie though, so they are barley worth the mention. Robin Williams is fantastic and even though there are some scenes that make you think 'what's the odds' it does a good job to say, maybe small, but there is a chance with this wacko. Okay, okay...I'm done, go see and enjoy the movie!</fullReview>

		<date>2002-09-02</date>
		<username>Chris Knipp</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mark Romanek creates a stark, scary world inhabited by a brilliant Robin Williams</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(Spoilers)`One Hour Photo' is a powerful little film whose stark perfection was laid out by writer-director Mark Romanek and secured by a courageous, edgy, disturbing performance by Robin Williams as Sy, the photo guy, the would-be uncle of the nice little family of Jake and Nina and Will Yorkin, whom he adopts through appropriating copies of their snapshots and mounding them all over a wall of his lonely apartment.Sy is a very isolated, very unsocialized, very sad man. He not only produces beautiful copies of the snapshots he's assigned to develop and print at the SavMart photo department: they are his sole existence. We don't learn why he has picked only this one family to identify with and plaster over his wall: this is an artificial assumption of the filmmakers. He could have appropriated other people's photos and fantasized about them too. Other customers are indicated whose lives, or occupations, Sy sees into, but the Yorkins are his single-minded obsession. Other than doing his job at SavMart and eating and sleeping and nursing this obsession, Sy has no life.What's scary about this movie is that nobody has a life, because we know even less about the others than about Sy. At times `One Hour Photo' becomes literally a monodrama, a play happening only inside its protagonist's head. Sy knows the Yorkins only from brief conversations and from what the camera has shown him. It's all superficial, like SavMart, like suburbia, like Sy's pretense of having a life.Another peculiarity of the story is that, sad and crippled though he is, Sy is a nice man and has only nice thoughts about Jake and Will and Nina. He admires and envies their apparently wholesome life and their lovely modernistic house, and he as much as tells them this when they come to the photo department or he runs into them. He even tells them - tossing it off almost as a joke - that he likes to think of himself as their uncle. And this is - almost - healthy. At least there's no dark concealment of even darker thoughts. When Sy approaches Jake at a boys' soccer game and offers him a toy, the scene is full of danger because it looks like pedophile stalking; but there's no indication that Sy's thoughts are anything but innocent - except that he's odd and loony to be feeding emotionally off people he barely knows. He's psychologically crippled, but has no twisted fantasies or awful impulses. He just wants to be normal and longs to enter the Yorkins' life. But this isn't possible. The situation is untenable.So after Sy finds flaws in this little family's world just when he's been fired and has nothing to lose, the craziness and violence latent in ultra-repressed Sy have to come out.Robin Williams' performance is riveting because the energy that has gone into it is all of implosion, of holding in, and without histrionics. Much of Sy's dialogue revolves around the Yorkins or is directed at them, so it's naturally pleasant and upbeat: Sy loves the Yorkins and he's at his best when doing something related to them. They are his life. But there's always an edge of desperation that grows from Sy's doomed efforts to ignore how empty his existence really is and how little he actually has to do with the Yorkins. Williams conveys an enormous sense of control which is reinforced by the antiseptic world the filmmakers give him to live in. This contributes to an almost unbearable mood of quiet tension that pervades the movie from first to last.Everything in this movie is visually styled: it's as obsessive-neurotic as its protagonist. When we're in the posh suburbs with the Yorkins, all the colors are Kodak bright. Everything in Sy's special world, his corner of SavMart and his downtown apartment, is `2001' white or cyanned-out gray, and his face is either blushing (with quickly repressed embarrassment or anger) or of an unhealthy pallor. All discussion, all symbolism, is of photo imagery or of seeing or surfaces. Sy is first seen being given mug shots in a police station even more antiseptic than SavMart. He speaks in voice-over of `snap shot' being originally a hunting term, and when he's trapped by police, his face is flattened by a bright light and he looks like a trapped animal. When he's trying to escape, he runs thorough a hall where an expert is lecturing on retinal transplants, and in his worst nightmare he imagines his eyes - which now have seen horror in the destruction of his dream - tortured and streaming blood. Perhaps Romanek's extensive background in music videos fed his obsessive pursuit of visual symbols in the movie.The effect is one of artificiality, though that adds to the starkness of the portrait. Even the scenes of the Yorkins together unseen by Sy are just a succession of clear Kodak Moments in saturated color. Later, it appears that a climactic sequence was only Sy's imagining. The movie neutralizes some of the horror we'd come to expect. Not everything about `One Hour Photo' seems fully alive and engaging. But Romanek shows a sense of stylistic force that's unusual. The movie is elegant like a mathematical proof. And it's not bloodless, because Robin Williams makes it come alive with his quite passionate performance. This is definitely one of the best and most powerful American movies of the year.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>One Hour Photo was visually interesting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo starring Robin Williams and directed by Mark Romanek was one of the films I eagerly anticipated once I heard that Robin did another 'dark' role after his work as an Alaskan serial killer in Insomnia. I wasn't disappointed. One Hour Photo's slow, almost snail-pace, storytelling, stunning use of color, startling imagery, and misguided dream sequences made me think of one director...the late Stanley Kubrick. I felt elements of The Shining, 2001: A Space Odyssey, and Eyes Wide Shut as Sy Parrish went about silently stalking, and living vicariously through, the Yorkin family. Robin does an excellent job just as he did in Insomnia and What Dreams May Come elevating him to the head of the 'comic actors' class (Jim Carrey, Tom Hanks, and Marlon Wayans being the others). While not necessarily a surprise ending, the climax did take me in a direction that most films of this nature just don't. You think you know what happens from the first 5 minutes on but the ending gives you a different spin on it. A very interesting case study in what happens to a soul without someone to love, One Hour Photo was definitely worth the price of admission.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-09-01</date>
		<username>Bear Graves</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Mark Romanek scores twice!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's been often said that a great painting can be judged just as much by the use of space surrounding the central subject as the subject itself. The same analogy can adjusted for cinema. Often it is the judicious use of silence that can carry as much of an impact in a movie as the dialog. "One Hour Photo" is such a film. Wearing two hats, that of writer and director, Mark Romanek has enough confidence in the power of his screen play to allow the principle editor (Jeffrey Ford) to set a languid pace to the film and utilize long stretches without chatter. This pace does not hinder the underlying creepiness of the film, on the contrary, the tension packs as slowly and surely as nitrogen in the tissues of a deep sea diver. The central character of the film is Cy Parrish "Cy, the Camera Guy!" played with consummate skill by Robin Williams. Parrish is a film developer that works in a one hour photo within a store that could be any Target or Wal-Mart. Having no life of his own and living day to day through the snap shots taken by others, he becomes enamored, and later, obsessed with a neighborhood family who's only contact with him are the photos that they drop off for development. A word here about Mr. Williams. I have never really considered him a good actor. He's played a multitude of roles and in each one you can see small bits of the Robin Williams "Shtick" peaking out from under the costume.(Another great example would be Chris Walken. I could watch him read a phone book, but have you ever seen him in a role where you didn't find yourself saying "Heh, heh.... Christopher Walken... Cool!"?). This movie changed all that. Rarely have I ever seen an actor submerge so completely his public mannerisms and persona. Robin Williams *was* Cy Parrish, the embodiment of the potential evil that can lurk under even the most banal of exteriors. Kudos are also due to Connie Nielson and Michael Vartan, who play the "twenty something" couple with son that becomes the unknowing object of Cy Parrish's obsession, as well as to Darrell Hall, the supervising music editor. Recommended, 8 out of 10 Bear Claws.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Shiva-11</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Beware the prints of darkness</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour PhotoBy Greg UrsicThou shalt not covet thy neighbour's wife or thy neighbour's goods. One needn't be religious to accept the wisdom of these words, which although penned over 5000 years ago, hold true today. While everyone likes to think they're above such pettiness, we've all experienced it? whether you're admiring your neighbour's new Porsche or your buddy's Baywatch clone girlfriend ? if only fleeting. In its mildest form these desires can lead to bitterness, at its worst, it may require a visit from the coroner.Sy Parrish is an unassuming nebbish whose life is upholstered in beige: friendless, clueless and stuck in a nowhere job (he's a photo clerk at the local SavMart ) he readily fades into the background. While the customers who bring their film in for developing barely acknowledge him, Sy has an all access pass into their most private moments. W hen someone tampers with his favorite happy shiny family, the Yorkins, Sy's not prepared to stand by and watch as his vision of perfection is sullied. It took Robin Williams - a Juilliard graduate and standup icon - nearly a decade to get accepted as more than a one trick pony (of course the drugs, alcohol and disastrous flops a la `Popeye' didn't help). With his breakthrough performance in The Dead Poets Society Hollywood finally began to take him serious and several acclaimed performances followed, culminating in his 1997 Oscar . Unfortunately the past few years saw Williams lapse into a succession of roles so banal and sickeningly sweet they had audiences reaching for their insulin. Whether or not this motivated his most recent choices ? deranged childrens show star in Death to Smoochy and killer in Insomnia ? is debatable (Williams claims it didn't). But with Sy, Williams reaches new heights (or more appropriately depths).Within moments of Sy's appearance you forget that you are watching Williams: there are none of the classic mannerisms, indeed Sy is hyper subdued. It is also quickly apparent that there is something seriously amiss; Sy is the atypical bad guy, guided not by money or global domination, but a desire to `do the right thing' (however warped that happens to be). It is a tribute to William's talent that as Sy's descent into the sinister grows the audience is simultaneously squirming in discomfort and feeling sorry for him. While the supporting cast helps to provide perspective, it is the physical elements of the film that qualify as the co-stars.The feel of the story is dramatically enhanced by the cinematography: the blandness of Sy's monochromatic existence appears even starker when juxtaposed next to the color flushed surroundings of the SavMart. Several shots mimic the through the lens view of a still camera, turning the viewer into a voyeur, and careful framing gives sections of the film a photo album quality . The final polish comes from the score which meshes flawlessly with the film, augmenting the dramatic elements without pandering to melodrama. If you see only one Robin Williams film this year, make it this one.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-08-26</date>
		<username>Jeremy Phillips (avenger_fan@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Some Day My Prints Will Come (SPOILERS)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sy Parrish is a quiet, introverted technician at a busy photo lab. For the past decade he has been developing the pictures for an ordinary family, and, with the passing of time, he has gradually become obsessed with becoming a permanent part of their lives...I had been looking forward to seeing this film. A change of direction is exactly what Robin Williams needs to maintain his creative vitality, and having seen documentaries about his work in music videos, I greatly anticipated seeing a feature film directed by Mark Romanek.The opening act is carefully judged and virtually note-perfect. Parrish is shown as pedantic about creating the finest set of prints possible, this care and attention being balanced by his lonely existence. He eats, and lives, alone, and Romanek provides the natural ending to this segment with a long shot of Sy sitting in his near-empty apartment watching television, with the wall behind him covered in duplicate prints of the Yorkin family's pictures.The acting is excellent across the board. Advance reviews from Sundance had championed Williams, and rightly so: it's far and away the best "straight" performance he's ever delivered, streets ahead of the monotonous, sentimental goo he is normally required to emote. Nielson and Vartan are both good as the unwitting objects of Sy's psychosis, although Eriq La Salle appears still to be playing Dr. Peter Benton, his character in ER, failing to show sufficient distance to make Det. Van Der Zee a person rather than a plot convenience. Gary Cole is such obvious casting as Sy's boss, store manager Bill Owens, that somehow he feels entirely wrong in the part. It's as though the casting director's imagination was entirely sapped by putting Williams in the lead.The direction is atmospheric, and the photography elegant. Other than Sy's blood-soaked, yet antiseptic nightmare, one shot that in particular stayed in my mind was of Sy walking to his car, bathed in the sodium yellow of a street lamp. Heil and Klimek's music is subtle in how it evokes fear and unease without intruding on the audience's concentration. But the script is something else entirely.The film's ending was far too neat for my taste, given the build-up it had been given. Maya and Will did not seem sufficiently traumatised by their ordeal for it to have any lasting impact on themselves, their families or the viewers. The most disappointing aspect of Romanek's clever script was the final revelation that Sy had been abused and photographed as a child. This simple safety valve was so formulaic that it almost negated the originality of the rest of the film. Could Sy not have been driven insane by his years of isolation, solitude and looking at the pictures of others' happy times? This conceit drove the film from brave, virgin territory almost into the path of the mainstream and should have been more clearly thought out.One Hour Photo realises most of it's potential, and it is certainly one of the best films I have seen this year. I just feel annoyed that a film so bold and original could have such a contrived final twist. Had Sy's quiet madness been the result of living a nothing life, many of us in the audience might be left asking: what if it happened to the people we know? What if it happened to us?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The wasp on the jello</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo builds its stark intensity from "the little things" - brilliant nuances of expression and detail that elevate the film above the level of just another borderline-wacko-going-over-the-edge thriller, and Robin Williams gives an Oscar-worthy performance here, a hypnotic, masterfully etched portrayal of what it feels like - truly FEELS like - to eke out a traumatized existence of profound loneliness while still craving acceptance and human connection on the only level he can actually deal with it. This level, of course, gets him into trouble, and the most extraordinary thing about Williams' performance is that we UNDERSTAND this tormented character, we understand why he does what he does, and we sympathize. But Sy gets our sympathy not because Williams tries to get it superficially (from the outside), but rather because he lets us IN - he lets us FEEL his vulnerability, his rage, his monumental sadness, his overwhelming need, and by the end of the movie we have experienced something truly rare: somebody else's reality. And we have recognized the part of ourselves that, chillingly, relates.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-08-25</date>
		<username>Jerry Langford</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Peeping Mork</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sy Parrish (Robin Williams) is a philosopher of photography. The odd part is that he develops film at a one-hour-photo stand at "SavMart"--a giant chain store designed in the austere white-on-white of the space station in "2001." Working this nearly minimum-wage gig next to recent high-school grads, Sy has plenty of time to meditate on photography's relationship to mortality, memory and identity; the job seems more or less a cinch, when he isn't making duplicates of the family photos brought in by a lovely, absurdly photogenic soccer mom (Connie Nielsen). One might think that Sy had a Walter Mitty crush on the onetime Mrs. Gladiator; but then, Sy fixates on her adorable nine-year-old kid--a boy Sy wants to be his pretend nephew. (This being a Christine Vachon production, a creepout-inducing whiff of paedophilia is never too far away.) Then we go back to Sy's overemphatically empty apartment, where he sits watching a quite funny "Simpsons" episode with a sour, sober expression (so you know he's not right in the head), and the camera moves off Sy to reveal... a wall-sized shrine full of Connie Nielsen's family photos! Mom, Dad and Little Junior times a thousand! It's like Rupert Pupkin's roomful of cardboard cutouts given a slightly higher-tech twists There's an interesting, unpleasant, eerie thread in the middle of ONE HOUR PHOTO: the adulation of one stratum of American culture--the hopeless, Lotto-ticket-buying, fifty-year-old-who-makes -$25,000-a-year side--for another stratum: the new suburban family, rich and safe yet funky and sexy and not TOO white, bringing a little collegiate tang into American Beautyville. The Yorkins, the family Sy yearns to enter, suggest a late-nineties boomtime advertiser's wet dream: a $400,000 house and an SUV, yet they still buy vintage vinyl on ebay! (Or so one assumes.) The writer-director Mark Romanek paints the gap between Sy's world and the Yorkins' in not-too-subtle colors: Sy lives in uninhabited, austere, corporatized spaces that seem left over from Todd Haynes' SAFE, and the Yorkins live in an ickily warm tongue-bath of color--every second is a Kodak moment. (Sy's world, in which his blue SavMart blazer is the only lightning-bolt of color, at least has a freaky, Kubrick-derivative allure. The Yorkins' manse makes you want to run for the ghetto.) The movie is about Sy's attempt to "get over"--to realize the American dream of status, of belonging, of being like the family-friendly ad images that surround him all day. And, of course, that attempt is phrased in the uncolorful language of a horror movie. Essentially, ONE HOUR PHOTO is THE KING OF COMEDY given a HAND THAT ROCKS THE CRADLE Botox shot. Like DeNiro's Rupert Pupkin, Robin Williams' Sy wants in so bad that he ignores everything--everyday courtesy, the rules of his job, the desire not to appear to be a predatory paedophile, and the little bubble that Mrs. Yorkin would no doubt call her "personal space." But whose side is the movie on? Early on, Mr. Yorkin accuses the missus of overwhelming him with her demands for Jil Sander smocks and a new Mercedes sedan; late in the day, when Mr. Yorkin commits an awful and obvious pecadillo, Mrs. Y turns a blind eye to it. Does Romanek intend for us to think--as I suspect--that Mrs. Yorkin is more interested in her husband's credit cards than in being the nobly long-suffering wife? And if so, does that mean that Romanek intends the Yorkins to come across as they do--as Banana Republic replicants, 2-D nonentities who are less alive than their one-hour photos? Though it gets cheaper and cheesier with each passing half-hour, ONE HOUR PHOTO might've had a fleeting hauntingness if the man proudly wearing the blue SavMart vest were William H. Macy-- a master at painting the embattled dignity of small-potato white guys. As Sy, Robin Williams has been touted and lambasted for giving a most un-Williams-like performance. To me, this is the same Williams we see in JUMANJI and WHAT DREAMS MAY COME--humble, choked up, as deferential as Red Skelton, and dying to endear himself to us at every opportunity. One can feel the glee in the moment when Williams finally Goes Psycho, but it doesn't seem to come from anywhere within the character, or the actor. Is it possible that Robin Williams really doesn't have much of a dark side? His sentimentality seems to be closer to the bone than his recent streak of playing maniacs. I think Williams' best movie performance was in Michael Ritchie's criminally underrated 1983 comedy THE SURVIVORS: he could do his multi-character motormouth shtik in the context of a character as deranged as Sy. There, the derangement set him free. Here, Williams seems overimpressed by the notion that contrasting his typical nice-guy side with scenes of frothing lunacy is incredibly subversive and powerful. (Tom Hanks seems to have overcalculated the shock of seeing Tom Hanks shoot a man in the head in ROAD TO PERDITION.&gt; Like David Fincher, Mark Romanek floats about, head over heels, in a world where Aesthetic Equals Anaesthetic. As in Fincher's PANIC ROOM, there seems to be no directorial opinion as to who should win, or even who we should like; and this is not, I think, a Todd Haynes-like ambiguity. These filmmakers are simply more interested in the visual trimmings than in the traditional investments that power Hollywood storytelling. That can work when Fincher is at his peak--as in PANIC ROOM, where he breaks down a conventional thriller script into visual components as abstract as an Ad Reinhardt canvas. Romanek is nowhere near Fincher's level of mastery of craft; he seems to have issued one direction while making PHOTO--"Make it all look like 2001!"--and then kept his distance. He intentionally makes Sy's artisanship-cum-artistry metaphorically resonate in a number of different directions, but he seems to have no point of view about those resonances. Like most of his rock-video-god peers, he seems more a set dresser than a storyteller. Spike Jonze could give him some needed lessons in anti-style.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Scott Ramsay</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A nice development.......</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sy Parrish (Robin Williams) is the technician at the SavMart photo lab where he has worked for many years. Over time he has gotten to know many of the customers, but none more so than the Yorkin family. But just how far does Sy's obsession run?One Hour Photo is simply a stunning film; it's a tour de force of a movie that is not only a stunning debut movie (80's flick 'Static' not withstanding) from an exciting young director, but also boats a career best performance from Robin Williams.Williams is so convincing as Seymour 'Sy' Parrish that you completely forget that you are watching the man that tortured the movie watching world with the likes of 'Bicentennial Man', 'What Dreams May Come', 'Patch Adams', 'Father's Day', 'Jack', 'Flubber' and 'Mrs. Doubtifre'. But, I digress; it seems vulgar to mention such films in the same breath as this stunning performance. Williams builds on the work he did in Insomnia (which was actually filmed after this film, but you understand what I'm saying, right?) and brings to film history one of the finest portrayals of anonymous danger.Williams Sy is the guy you see every day, but never give more than a passing thought to. But, what if he is giving more than a passing thought to you? What if he has become so obsessed with your family that he starts turning up at your lunch break, or your kid's sports practice? Williams plays Sy perfectly, so ambiguous, so controlled. You're never sure what he's thinking behind those cold, cold eyes. His many voiceovers are delivered with an ice cold matter-of-fact manner that sends a chill down the spine. You're never quite sure what Sy is capable of, Williams never gives anything away and this creates tension from the very first frame. It's tension that continues to build slowly and deliberately throughout the film right up to the final frame. The tension practically drips out of the screen during the scenes where Sy 'bumps' into the various members of the family outside of the SavMart setting, you feel as uncomfortable as the family members, it's impressive stuff. Williams cold ambiguous manner coupled with Romanek's stark visual style leave the viewer unsettled and unsure what to expect next from the film. This certainly no by-the-numbers Hollywood stalker/slasher film, that much is certain.Romanek has teamed up with David Fincher's cinematographer of choice Jeff Cronenweth to produce a visually astounding movie. Although both Fincher and Romenek have a background in music videos, Fincher is a lot flashier than Romanek. Romanek's style is subdued, but stark and barren. The movie is beautifully filmed, with incredible use of light employed, whether it's in the sterile white photolab or the warm home of the Yorkin's. A stunning lighting effect is used in the final moments of the film to ground Sy in one of his earlier voiceovers. It's an incredible moment and one that will live with me for a good while.I look forward to Romaneks next film as after his work here he can now be mentioned in the same breath as the likes of Soderbergh, PT Anderson, Fincher and Nolan as a new hot director to watch out for.Williams steals this film, but let's not do disservice to the rest of the cast who step up to the plate and give some admirable work. Connie Nielsen is perhaps the best on offer; she works well as the mother of the Yorkin family. I like Michael Vartan in TV show 'Alias' and it's nice to see him in a film, he is given little to do until later in the film, but he handles some difficult scenes well. Dylan Smith has little to do, but be suitably cute as the Yorkin's son.It's always nice to see Gary 'Boss from Hell' Cole and he again plays a boss here. He had better watch out though, or he'll become stereotyped! Also, apparently there is life after ER as Eriq La Salle pops up later in the film and has some impressive scenes with Williams.The supporting players however, are just the lettuce at the side of the cheeseburger that is Robin Williams. I cannot stress how good he is here; it's easily his best work to date, surpassing the likes of 'The World According To Garp', 'Good Will Hunting', 'The Fisher King', 'Dead Poet's Society' and the recent 'Insomnia'. This is an Oscar worthy performance, but I fear that the Academy might shirk at the films dark subject matter and ambiguous nature. The Academy likes easy films, which One Hour Photo is certainly not.In-fact One Hour Photo is a cold, dark, ambiguous film that leaves the viewer unsure of its direction for the entire running time. It asks questions of the viewer constantly and leaves itself burned into the viewers conscience long after the end titles have rolled and the lights have came up.9/10 for One Hour Photo.</fullReview>

		<username>rivetbadtz (rivetbadtz@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the most visionary and important films ever</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Im sure my customers never think about it, but these snapshots are their little stands against the flow of time...all that stands between us and oblivion"-Robin Williams, OHPI have just seen an advance screening of One Hour Photo, which literally left me in awe...the kind of feeling you got the first time you saw Se7en or The Game. There's just those rare films that come along once in a great while, a moment out of time masterpiece. This is one of those extrordinary films. After a year of buzz and waiting, I saw it and...quite simply, Mark Romanek has crafted one of the most visionary and important films on the American cinematic landscape today. In Robin Willaims most brilliant role since Toys(1992), we see one of the bravest performances of a lifetime. How to describe OHP? With heavy shades of Harmony Korine(namely still photos with narrations) and Kubrick(lot sof Kubrick!) OHP in lamens terms could be called Red Dragon with a dash of Office Space and The Game. Robin Williams is at once Lecter-cum-Walt Whitman intellect and sad recluse. Williams plays sy Parrish, a guy who phillosophizes over episodes of The Simpsons and obsesses over collecting Neon Genesis Evangelion anime figures. He is living through a decade worth of yorkin family photos, trying to belong...through dreaming and fantasy, meticulously finishing his final masterpiece.Sy Parrish wants to be accepted, desperately living life through the most innate and smallest of details. Mark Romanek's stunning visual cinematography and his attention to the smallest of details is remarkable, as Romanek fills the screen with the most ornate of images and Aronofsky like inner working closeups. Romanek paces his film with master precision, giving us standing attention to his subject and where he wants us to go. We sympathize with Sy, as the world has been shattered for him. This is more than just a portrait of a man at his breaking point, living in his own reality...this is also a study on the family structure, and a lesson in great cinematic filmaking in general.Romanek uses not broad strokes, but powerful stains of symbolic and surreal dreamlike visuals. If youve seen his avant garde videos for Madonna's Betime Stories or Nine Inch Nails Closer you know what I mean. In fact, Trent Reznor did a brillaint job on the score, a minimalist yet intense ambience that provides an effective backbone.I am already calling this the best film of 2002, and when OHP hits theatres this Fall, movie goers will be in for quite a treat. What is going to be one of the most talked about films of the year, Willians and Romanek surely need to be frontrunners for Oscars next year. One Hour Photo is not to be missed.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2002-01-19</date>
		<username>rehj</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>One Hour Photo delivers in the end.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One Hour Photo is a thriller of a different sort. While the buzz is about Robin Williams taking on a dark, malevolant role, the substance is a unexpectedly honest look into the mind of a stalker who perceives himself as anything but.Mark Romanek puts the audience through a variety of paces, forcing us to re-evaluate our opinion about Seymour (Sy) Parrish throughout. Williams performance transcends himself, and allows the viewer to forget who the actor is and focus on the story within the first 1/2 hour. He is uncharacteristically subdued, and often leaves you wishing for more animation and insight, though this is obviously deliberate, and ultimately a boon to the film.The story develops around Sy's peripheral involvement in Nina (Connie Nielsen) and Will (Michael Vartan) Yorkin's and their son's life, via years of developing their family photos. Sy quietly adopts the Yorkin's as his own family, but what starts as a benign fixation is slowly revealed as much more, in remarkably subtle, but twisted examples. While we are immediately told that Sy has done something wrong at the beginning of the film, Romanek leads us on a red-herring filled chase to the end.Nielsen gives a realistic portrayal of an unhappy housewife in a financially successful marriage, though Vartan comes off paper thin throughout, particularly in a rather stilted marital tiff designed to clue us in on the state of the Yorkin's life. The son, uncredited here, provides the biggest dramatic peril, as he immediately connects with the viewer, but ultimately his competition for screentime with his mother blunts the emotional impact intended.Romanek's unique approach to staging, color choice, and costuming all lend to a surreal view into his characters everyday life. While his approach to symbolism can be heavy handed at times, at others it is equally subtle, and effective in either case.Despite these shortcomings, One Hour Photo delivers in the end, leading to a stunning climax uncommon in today's Hollywood fare. One Hour Photo is a leap into a new approach in audience manipulation, character insight, and selective plot development. A strong effort, that may come up lacking in box office success, but will certainly find its audience in video sales, and overseas distribution.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Aaron Takhar</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A frightening portrait of life on the edge of madness</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robin Williams's mysterious character creates an unnerving story line which is visually successful in this extremely creepy thriller. The story deals with a theme which is not at all unique but still touched on which is loneliness. Many films show the struggle that the protagonist has to go through; in particular it's is an ideology shared with young men such as Travis Bickle in "Taxi Driver" and the nameless narrator in "Fight Club". The film draws on how our society is capable of excluding people who are unable to articulate their feelings. Whilst watching it I enjoyed how visually through some of the unusual shots of surreal lighting it managed to captivate me and I was in Seymour Parrish's world. Overall the film was an excellent portrayal of a character that is deeply disturbed and falls further into insanity. The concept is simple, Seymour lives his life and his opinions are made up through the snap shots of others. It shows us how the emptiness in his life is fulfilled by that of others, as in this world it is safe compared to his life. Deep down he knew it was flawed and admitted that when you take photos it is misleading into someone's life as pain is hardly shown. He realises his urban alienation halfway through the film and attempts to rectify the situation which leads to its ending.Moments of the film which make it stand out to other psychological films can be considered as strange or even disturbing; the prime example of this is his dream. Director Mark Romanek cleverly uses the blood out of Sy's (Seymour's) eyes to show the pain he has experienced when he realises the family he observes isn't as perfect as he first thought. The blood is symbolic of how is blinded in pain and confusion, the surreal white glow makes it more haunting. The film in my opinion was also successful because of the performance of Robin Williams. He is underrated as a serious actor and although he has thrived as a comedic actor in "Mrs Doubtfire" and "Good Morning Vietnam", it is very harsh to overlook his serious performances. In both "Awakenings" and "Insomnia" he was excellent at supporting two humongous actors in De Niro and Pacino. But in One Hour Photo he is the lead and offers a hauntingly powerful performance and conveys all sorts of emotion in an extremely convincing manner and really should off at least been nominated for an academy award and really should of won it!Mark Romanek creates an extremely uncomfortable portrait of life on the edge of madness. Romanek also wrote this impressive script which doesn't rely on shock value or an obscure out-of-context twist unfitting to time, culture or irrespective to sociological points of view which just attempt to bamboozle the audience, instead like Paul Schrader's amazing script of Taxi Driver it simply presents a more realistic view of life. A bit like Travis Bickle or Trevor Reznik in "The Machinist" he has a strong self -destruct mechanism and can be labelled as pathetic at his attempt to articulate to other people and interact with what could be considered as "normal" people. This intense drama which at the flaws of the society we live in this extremely captivating tale of loneliness and an in-depth character study of someone who on the edge who is ready to be pushed. Overall it's an immensely powerful drama which can be considered a cult classic and should be watched by any fan of films which tackle more difficult subjects such as observing how society is capable of rejection in Mark Romanek's stunning film.</fullReview>

		<username>BA_Harrison</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Robin Williams develops an obsession and exposes his dark side.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Zany comedic actor Robin Williams goes against type to star as slightly unhinged photo technician Seymour 'Sy' Parrish, who over the years has formed an unhealthy obsession with the seemingly perfect family of suburban wife and mother Nina Yorkin (Connie Nielsen), a regular customer at his lab.When Sy loses his job, and then discovers that Nina's husband Will (Michael Vartan) is sleeping with work colleague Maya Burson (Erin Daniels), the loner's fantasy of becoming part of a perfect family unit is shattered, and he embarks on a crusade to expose the affair and punish the guilty parties.A tense study of a fractured psyche brought about through severe childhood trauma and long term social inadequacy, One Hour Photo provides its talented star with the role of a lifetime, one that requires the usually madcap performer to be, at turns, likable, pitiable, creepy and absolutely chilling. The amazingly versatile Williams pulls off this trick with seeming ease, giving a thoroughly convincing performance throughout.Writer/director Mark Romanek also deserves much praise for his sharp script and stylish visuals, but should be particularly commended for not succumbing to Hollywood-itis: his main character isn't just another Hannibal Lector or Patrick Bateman, but rather a very real individual, deserving of compassion and understanding. This fact seems to bother some reviewers here on IMDb, who clearly wanted the guy to become a total monster and go on a killing spree at the end of the film, but I'm glad Romanek took the more subtle route?it makes the film a refreshingly different and more satisfying experience overall.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>One of the most original thrillers in recent years.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"One Hour Photo" uses an original concept and has an original approach.The story is about real-life and real-life characters. The thriller elements in the movie don't really ever feel forced and the movie actually has a pretty realistic atmosphere and certain tension. It's mainly thanks to the actors that the movie works out this way.Guess that no one ever really thought about it but the guy who develops your photos for years, knows more about you and your personal life than you would really want to. This movie shows what can happen if a developer gets obsessed with your personal life and feels that he is- or at least should be a part of your 'normal' life.I'm probably making the movie sound more 'thriller-like' than it in fact is. Yes, "One Hour Photo" is a thriller but not one with constant suspension or an haunting atmosphere.This is probably due to the approach of the movie, that is original and refreshing but also is a killer for the much needed formulaic thriller ingredients. The main character of the movie is the 'psychophate' himself. Instead of seeing him as a monster, you can't help feeling compassion for him. Sy Parrish is a lonely guy but kind guy and everything that he has in the world is his job. He has never been married and is probably a single all his life and he has no family. His loneliness and sadness becomes very sensible in the movie and you really start to feel compassion for the character and want him to be happy. In that regard "One Hour Photo" is probably a better dramatic character movie than a thriller.It's absolutely thanks to Robin Williams that the main character works out so well in the movie. He's highly convincing in this serious role. Somewhat of course he very rarely ever plays. He really is the one that carries the movie and here is also were one of the problems of the movie lays. All of the other characters are pushed to the back. It doesn't make the story work out as powerful and tense as it could had been. The Yorkin's are a pretty good movie-family but their fears and their reactions toward the weird- and behavior of Sy at certain points of the movie gets highly underdeveloped. It's probably the reason why it isn't the most effective thriller around, though it's absolutely an original and great one to watch.The storytelling is for most part good. It takes its time to draw out the character and set up the situations. It shows how the character slowly slips from being obsessed, to being a dangerous and psychotic stalker, when things are turning against him. It's definitely true that the movie goes a bit overboard toward the ending when everything happens fast and there is build up to an ending that just can't life up to its build up.It's definitely a well directed movie with a good visual style and some great shot and effective sequences, though Mark Romanek obviously still has a lot of learning to do. But the basis is here already notable in the movie and the basis is real good, so that promises a lot for his future movies.Highly recommendable due to its originality and performances from the cast but just don't watch this movie expecting a tense stalker-thriller, that will bring you to the edge of the seat.7/10http://bobafett1138.blogspot.com/</fullReview>

		<fullReview>We know how a psycho behaves. He stuffs his dead mother, he keeps his victims prisoners for months in a well, he has a creepy dungeon full of perverted stuff, he has an invisible friend, he invents deadly cat-and-mouse games. This is the psycho Hollywood has taught us about for decades. But the real psycho, the one you'd never notice, the one which can pass for normal for years and years, where is he in movies? In One Hour Photo.This movie and Insomnia is what i call "Robin Williams goes serious". Instead of his cheery, lovable characters he usually portrays, he comes off as a cool, slightly abnormal person living on the edge of insanity. While Kevin Spacey has the charisma of a person who'd kill his mother without a blink of an eye, Robin Williams is the guy who everybody thinks is normal and who in every way is, until he snaps. He was a lonely writer in Insomnia; here he's a lonely photo-lab worker.Williams is great in his role. Jack Nicholsson was suggested for the movie, and while I love Jack, I can't help feeling he would have ruined the tension and the subtle parts of Sy's character. Robin Williams is perfect in the role and you forget it's him ever so often.The movie is technically brilliant, it stays away from the clichés, the script is tight and the supporting cast is pretty good(except the child actor, but that's to be expected). The pacing is pretty slow but none the less gripping and engaging.Very few movies are like this. So be prepared to see something original when you watch this. It's always nice with a breeze of fresh air in movies today.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>While this movie was neither a huge box office success nor is one of Robin William's more popular films, I found it to be very well-made and realistic.Most thrillers nowadays are very far-fetched and often tend to portray the antagonist as a one-dimensional psycho. This movie was very believable and did a terrific job of portraying Robin William's character Sy Parrish as a sympathetic but very disturbed figure. It is really hard to hate his character who seemingly just wanted to have a family of his own.Throughout the movie, you learn that he is very lonely, has no family and loves his job as a photo tech. You also learn that has become obsessed with one of his customers and her family, has illegally amassed tons of their family photos and frequently stalks them outside their home. As the movie progresses, Sy becomes more disgruntled until he eventually snaps. Even when he ultimately snaps, you still kind of feel bad for him in a way.While it may be argued that nothing was really resolved in the ending, I thought it was very appropriate. I really liked the fact that neither Sy (nor anyone else for that matter) was killed in the end. A lot of other movies will usually kill the villain because it is generally believed that he deserved to die. But that wasn't the case here as Sy was a very complex character.Lastly I felt that this movie was not overly long and really flowed well. It's one of the few movies on home video that I can actually watch without hitting the stop or pause button at least once.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fantasy Land</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>******SPOILERS****** Sy Parrish, Robin Williams, working at the local SavMat as a film developer at it's One Hour Photo store besides developing film has developed a very strong attachment to one of his customers, the Yorkin family trying to fit into their world as Uncle Sy. Sy secretly duplicates every one of the Yorkin family photos for himself and in his apartment has an entire wall made up of those photos. To Sy the Yorkin's seem to be the family that he didn't have when he was growing up as a child and now living alone yearns for. There's times when Sy drives to the Yorkin home and sits in his car for hours fantasizing about living with them as their favorite Uncle Sy. One afternoon Sy is called into the SavMat managers Bill Owen's, Gary Cole, office and told that there hundreds of photos that he processed that are not accounted for and because of that he will have to let him go at the end of the week. Hurt over the loss of his job and not having the chance to have contact with the Yorkin's that night looking at all the photos of the Yorkin's that he has Sy sees in a photo of some kind of reunion and a woman in it that looks very familiar to him. The woman turns out to be one of Sy's customers, Erin Daniels, Maya Burson, at the Photo Mat. Checking on Erins photos Sy sees that she's having an affair with Mr. Yorkin, Michael Vartan behind Mrs. Yorkin's Connie Nielsen, back! Overnight Sy changes from a harmless eccentric into an unstable as well as dangerous individual. Sy's actions that are set off by what he found out about Mr. Yorkin's infidelities ends up landing him in the police station where we first saw him at the beginning of the movie. It's also here in the police station that you get the most revealing insight in what Sy is all about. Going into a long and emotional speech about broken families and child abuse at first you think that Sy is mindlessly blabbering. It's alter then it starts to dawn on you that Sy is talking about his own childhood and the suffering and abuse that he went through. You also see that his actions against Mr. Yorkin and Miss. Daniels was to shame them of what they were doing to Mrs. Yorkin and most of all their nine year old son Jake, Dylan Smith. As if it reminded Sy of his own troubled childhood and his attempt to avoid that from happened to Mrs. Yorkin and especially little Jake whom Sy has taken a very strong liking for. As well as Jake looking up to Sy as some kind of friend or big brother.You begin to understand that whatever Sy did was because of a kind of misplaced ethics that he developed in his own growing up and almost forgive, like detective Van Der Zee, Eriq La Salle whom he's being interrogated by, him for what he did. Just before Det. Van Der Zee is about to leave the interrogation room Sy tells him meekly if he can see the photos that he took during the actions that put him in the situation that he's in now.Det. Van Der Zee sympathetically and understandingly hands them over to Sy. As Sy opens the envelope that the photos are in and spreads them over on the table you can see that Sy not only lost his job and his freedom. You begin to realize that the poor and troubled man who did what he did in his convoluted self righteous actions may have lost his mind as well! or did he! Sy cleverly shamed the two adulterers into silence by making them think what he was doing not by what what he actually did! Robin Williams gives a performance in "One Hour Photo" of Sy Parrish that would be hard to top by any actor around today.</fullReview>

		<username>MovieAddict2016</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>You won't be developing your photos again!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"One Hour Photo" is exactly what I expected. A very well made, entertaining chiller-thriller that is memorable and notable, but not altogether one of the best thrillers ever. It's one of those that people will be talking about for about a year, but will then vanish, much like the other thrillers about psychopathic psychos.Robin Williams is the psycho in this film, named Sy Parrish, a.k.a. Sy the Photo Guy, as he is called by the son of a family he develops photos for at SavMart, which the director described as a kind of "Target meets K-Mart." Though the obvious similarity is Wal-Mart.Sy takes his job at SavMart personally--very personally. He's the one hour photo technician. And, as he narrates to us in the beginning, most people do not realize that photo developers actually look at the pictures you have taken when they develop them. What if one of these onlookers was a complete oddball? Weirdo? Psycho? What if? Let's say the guy makes an extra copy of your photos, just for him to take home and study. Soon that guy starts to think he is actually part of your family. That's when things really start to go haywire. He comes into your life, doing bizarre things. This technique has been used before, but what makes "One Hour Photo" so much better is its main character, Sy. He has feelings, emotions, and thoughts. Most slasher-flicks are about little crazy guys that we can't relate to, but you feel for Sy, and, as he does a few things, you start to believe what he is doing--though odd--is kind of good.Robin Williams is getting critical acclaim for giving a "terrifying performance." For a moment, I'm going to pretend I know what that means. To me, Williams does not give a terrifying performance. He gives an excellent, scary, but deep performance; as I mentioned above, he's more than some shallow slasher with a knife going after everyone. He has emotions, feelings, thoughts, and we can see them on the surface.I thoroughly enjoyed "One Hour Photo." The directing is classic, the acting talent is superb, and I guess I'm probably the only one who thought Williams' character is actually kind of the good guy. He reminds me of Michael Douglas in "Falling Down": His bubble has burst, and he's serving up his own justice. What Williams' character, Sy, does at the end of the film, is actually kind of justified.And that's what makes this movie so different from all the other "psychological thrillers" out there.4/5 stars -John Ulmer</fullReview>

I couldn't sympathize with the family, and it all seemed to be a glossy and neat style of cinematography trying to cover bad writing. Psychological thrillers come a lot better than this.</fullReview>

		<username>jimboduck</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Best of Recent Robin Williams ? 8 (Bright, clean, and nervous like a straight jacket)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I think of all the recent Robin Williams movies I've seen, I'm left with a peculiar aftertaste. He appears in stylish, well produced films that don't really leave a lasting impression. Foremost in my mind are What Good Things Will Come, Bicentennial Man, and Final Cut, none of which I would recommend. They're all too busy.One Hour Photo stands out from the rest because unlike the others it's simple and effective. Williams does a thoroughly convincing job as Cy the Photo Guy, a character who epitomizes the privately obsessive side of human nature. That side we strive to squash on a daily basis to appear presentable in public.The effectiveness of simplicity shines through in every aspect of ONE HOUR PHOTO. The score is creepy. The photo lab is antiseptic. Williams' strained mannerisms suggest that he has too many secrets brooding in the subconscious. And the plainness of the local retail store and soccer-mom-suburbia confirms the suspicion that the neighborhood is too comfortable. The undercurrent of psychosis lurks precisely where life appears so ordinary.I was lost on how such a widely known Robin Williams movie could affect me so deeply, but the background of the director provided some insight. Mark Romanek hasn't worked on too many feature-length films, and most of his work has been producing music videos. That makes sense, because ONE HOUR PHOTO features a lot of well placed color against sterilized white. I don't know how to put it. It's the silent kind of creepy when you're watching a mime. It's like watching a skilled surgeon operating on a patient's leg. Camera zooms out to reveal that he's working on his own leg! (credit arnold for that one)Here's a shortlist of music videos by Romanek you can find on his 2005 Director's Label DVD:JAY-Z - 99 Problems; RED HOT CHILI PEPPERS - Can't Stop; JOHNNY CASH - Hurt; AUDIOSLAVE - Cochise; NO DOUBT - Hella Good; NIN - Perfect Drug; BECK - Devil's Haircut; EELS - Novocaine for the Soul; MICHAEL &amp; JANET JACKSON - Scream; MADONNA - Bedtime Story; LENNY KRAVITZ - Are You Gonna Go My Way; EN VOGUE - Free Your Mind; KD LANG - Constant CravingWhile a noisy production could have driven you away, the simplicity of ONE HOUR PHOTO draws you in. Watch this movie if you're tired with THX explosions, if you're losing faith in Robin Williams, or if you're looking for some good old, quiet perversion.JYJimboduck-dot-com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"All I did was take pictures..."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Each time you go to a discount department store, you see familiar faces. There's the door greeter, the woman behind the snack bar, the usual cash register attendants. The movie One Hour Photo uses this idea, namely the man who processes the film at a one-hour photo lab in a Wal-Mart-esquire retail store in Suburbia, USA. Robin Williams in one of those roles that continues to tell audiences he's more than Mork in his acting ability plays Sy the Photo Guy, a harmless looking man who is painfully lonely and decides to adopt a family, whether they know it or not. The Yorkin family is a loving family of 3, with father Will (Michael Vartan) and Nina (Connie Neilsen), with son Jake (Dylan Smith). Every time pictures are taken, they take them to Sy to have them developed. Sy, while processing the film, makes copies for himself to take home and "live" life with the Yorkins. This movie is powerful in showing that creepy, despicable characters are sometimes the ones we have the most sympathy for. Robin Williams conveys a feeling pity when he is doing the most wrong. One Hour Photo, written and directed by Mark Romanek, makes a familiar guy in all of our lives and makes him a parent's worst nightmare. He's just a lonely guy trying to make it in this world, but he makes bad choices. The only character who sees the bad side of Sy early is his boss, Bill, played by Gary Cole of Lundbergh fame in Office Space--sadly, no TPS reports here in One Hour Photo, a much more serious and somber tone to the film. All of the actors are top notch, but it's Williams who performs the best, making a horrible human being a sympathetic creep by the end of the film.</fullReview>
