		<shortDescription>Nice tidy little thriller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a tightly constructed and well executed thriller with an outstanding performance from an actor who has often been in danger of being strangled by his own mannerisms. Lennon and McCartney asked the question: "All the lonely people ? where do they all come from?' Seymour Parrish (Sy) is a case in point; he seems to have come from nowhere, no family no friends, just his job as a photo finisher inside Save Mart, a massive discount store in a suburban shopping mall. He takes his work seriously and he cultivates his customers, particularly the Yorkins, an attractive young couple with a cute eight-year old son called Jake. Sy becomes obsessed by the Yorkins who seem to have the perfect family life he has been denied and he fantasises about becoming part of it. Then he discovers, in the line of duty so to speak, that Will Yorkin is having an affair with a workmate. Sy is sacked (dismissal has been looming for some time) and over the edge he goes.Robin Williams as Sy reminded me a little of Rod Steiger's Mr Joyboy, the mortician in "The Loved One", a pedantic minor functionary with a very well developed sense of his own importance. Unusually for Williams it's a very restrained performance, as it must be; Robin Williams would not have remained employed at the Mart for 11 minutes, let alone 11 years. Even when Sy cracks, it is more of an implosion than an explosion.Two less well-known actors with non-American backgrounds, Connie Nielsen and Michael Vartan play Nina and Will Yorkin (no doubt "your kin"). They put in fine performances, but their low profiles help emphasis their generic character ? they are ordinary anonymous suburbanites - they could be you or me. Bigger names would have found this harder to carry off. The other notable performance is from Gary Cole as Bill, Sy's boss, a typical nasty middle manager basically unconcerned about his staff.The "Save Mart" (read "Wal Mart") is monstrous, impersonal, bathed in fluorescent light and devoted to profit, and not a place anybody of sensitivity would want to work. For that matter the Los Angeles suburbs it was set in are pretty awful ? the crew had to go to Brentwood, near San Francisco to find the Yorkins' designer house. Strangely, until things fly apart Sy seems remarkably well adapted to the Mart, as if his very lack of other involvement makes it his home ? he is institutionalised. As to what is motivating him, he has a little rant towards the end that suggests he might be taking revenge for some earlier abuse he suffered, but it's not clear why the Yorkins were the target.I guess you could say the film deplores adultery, though for once the punishment is not death. In fact I appreciated the lack of gore. Lonely people aren't all serial killers, after all. As to where they come from, well we don't really care, do we?</fullReview>

		<date>2004-10-06</date>
		<username>foxgirl</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Picture perfect.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The entire time I watched this movie, I felt as though I were looking at an endless series of snapshots. Everything was too perfect and clean, and the colours too crisp and pure, the store too vast and white and gleaming... but not enough to make the surreality of the setting obscure the story. Everything's just this side of reality--just like when, for example, you see the photos of someone that they want to show you, the best ones. None of the ones where their hair is messed-up, their expression silly, their rear shot at an unflattering angle can be found--there's nary a single shot in this masterpiece out of place. As Sy's voice-over says, if you look through a person's photo albums, you might come away with the impression that nothing bad ever happens to them. In addition to including the key colours of photo development and emphasizing Sy's sterile, lonely identity with the whites, beiges and taupes of his environments, we are placed artistically in a world that can only be described as picture-perfect.I could go on about the cinematography, but that's not all I want to rave about. Robin Williams' performance in Insomnia was like a dress rehearsal for this film--as Sy, he gives us even more of a subtle, spare view into an unstable character's world. With each troubled role he takes on, he performs better and better. True aficionados of character acting will not at all be disappointed by this film... and yes, it sounds strange to me, too, to place Robin Williams in that esteemed acting category. He lives up to the praise he has garnered for this movie, and then some.**SPOILERS BELOW**I've read criticisms of the tensions between Mr. and Mrs. Yorkin--they fight over money, but she spends all her time at the discount store, Savmart. I feel that those who espouse this notion are missing some serious subtleties in the Yorkins' relationship--she feels that he has emotionally distanced himself from the family, and he seems to be expressing misplaced frustration with himself and the situation when he argues with her over her spending. Multiple instances in the movie bear out both complaints--we find he is having an affair, and we discover that Mrs. Yorkin has 'some serious shopping to do' during the day at the mall, and is consistently dressed and coiffed like a model, somewhat out of the norm for your average Savmart customer. It's a very realistic fight between a husband and wife about... nothing that they're actually voicing at all.As to whether or not the nude, posed photos were actually ever taken... well, I, for one, am satisfied to be left without that question being definitively answered. Does it matter? Mr. Yorkin and Maya were both duly humiliated, which was part of the purpose of the exercise. Perhaps it's better if he didn't, if he took pictures of random objects in their room, in his own--it would make us even slightly more sympathetic to Sy. But regardless, the ending makes it fairly clear that whatever the reality of it, Sy was acting against the two of them with, as a previous reviewer so eloquently put it, the worst punishment in his world.It's a picture... act like you're enjoying it. Act like you're having fun. And at the end of it, you're left feeling guilty and dirty and ashamed and angry... somewhat like the way the movie lets you feel at the end. I can't tell you how much I enjoy movies that really live up to Traffic's tagline--"No one comes away clean." Requiem for a Dream was like that, Traffic itself is like that, and One Hour Photo is certainly like that. I think it's a good thing, having been a retail lackey, to have some people realize what it is like to be looked through, to be looked over. And likewise, it's a good thing for others to realize that beautiful people in perfect homes assure their children and themselves that everyone's fine, they have girlfriends and mommies and daddies who love them. Both sets of people could benefit from a viewing of this film, but no one's going to come away with a good and light-hearted feeling when the credits begin to roll.I respect that, in a film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Sy Parrish's life is dull. Now, we've all seen dull people before, but with Sy, it's painfully dull. Don't do what I think you're going to do!"And the revelation at the end is absolutely spot-on. Absolutely, totally brilliant.I put off seeing this film for a long time, as I had seen "Insomnia" and didn't care for it, and thought this would be the same sort of character. Far from it!Oh, by the way, Mark -- I saw the riderless horse as symbolizing futility and pointlessness, like Sy's life. It's interesting, the different interpretations of things!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A lot to learn in this movie...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of the best all around movies I have ever seen.First the acting is superb with several actors. Williams is number one in this film; his abilities shine brightly in these more serious rolls. The young boy played by Dylan Smith is wonderful in the role. You get a loose idea of who these people are and using your sleuthing skills you form your own idea of who this family is. This allows the story to effectively portray the more important things in this film.You will be sitting on the edge of your seat wanting to turn this movie off. It'll rip your stomach up and repulse you. This is all good thou. The strength of this story helps make it chilling and suspenseful, you feel as if you are the one being attacked, as if you are the family in this movie. It'll hurt and make you afraid. Fighting to not press the stop button is what made this movie enjoyable, it felt REAL.The director went one extra step in making the suspenseful atmosphere that more influencing on you. The camera work is amazing. One can notice that they selected each color specifically to match the mood of that scene and portray the feelings of each character. The bright light and white and blue color all over the store portrays mood of sterile and safe. It gives you the idea that this is a safe place to be, you would never expect anything to go wrong here. The dark green tint used in certain scenes gives this un easy feeling, portraying an under lying pain in Sy... a poison. Just take a look at the still picture at the end of the film and see the brilliant photography used for the shot. All the colors and lighting can describe exactly what kind of people these are.This is a thinking movie, a movie you have to just sit back and absorb every thing in it and figure out a lot on your own. To see where everyone is coming from. If you are into art films or thinking movies watch this. And I also believe if your a film student, you can learn a lot about how the camera tells another story then the script... the camera can talk too.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>3 Stars: Intelligent showcase for Robin Williams</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>***:The film is an intelligent but a more-or-less forgettable outing highlighted by Robin William's against-type performance as the creepy central character, Cy. Cy, a film developer at a one-hour photo department, has an obsession with his favorite clients, the Yorkins and for every roll of film they send in, he keeps an extra copy for his own personal use. Additionally, one of the walls of Cy's is decorated with a humongous mural of all the pictures the family has ever sent for development and Cy secretly spies on the family and takes pictures. Cy, however, is very passive and actually quite pleasant with his interactions to his coworkers, his boss, and the Yorkin family. On the other hand, however, he scares us to death, and that more than anything is a tribute to William's enormous range. That's the paradox of Williams' character: as opposed to the guy who cheats on his wife, Williams is really doing nothing wrong out of a cultural context for a society that values privacy so highly. A statement is also being made here about our society's premium on conformity and the hypocrisy of that. The Yorkins are hypocritical because they devote their time to producing images of themselves as a happy and loving family, which of course they are not, making Cy a sort of truth bringer, doing nothing but exposing the hypocrisy of that. Even more so, the hypocrisy comes to light within ourselves when we're watching Cy get fired and empathize with the guy doing the firing. Thus, the movie is thematically very clever and thanks to Robin Williams, genuinely convincing. I would've rated higher if the script might have upped the level of fear so the story could have moved at a more exciting pace</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>laursene</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>NOW I know who Robin Williams reminds me of ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's Jonathan Harris! Yes, Dr. Smith from Lost in Space. A lot of people don't remember that earlier in his career the comic Harris specialized in sinister characters - one of his creepiest roles being as a doctor on a classic Twilight Zone episode.This is the Harris that Williams is channeling in One Hour Photo. The resemblance is uncanny: The widely-set, beady eyes and somewhat irregular mouth that can seem either emotionally urgent or blocked off depending on the situation. Both play characters who seem to be hiding something, and when they let it out, it's something pretty strong: blind rage, desperate need, terror, deep loneliness. The latter, of course, was the running subtheme of Lost in Space, followed closely by terror. Harris's relationship with the Robinson family, particularly the son Will, parallels Williams' relationship here with the Yorkins and especially the son, Jake. The overly bright, suggestively "alien" lighting of the various interiors in One Hour Photo also parallel the eerie lighting of the spaceship in Lost in Space.I'm not arguing here that Lost in Space was great TV, or that One Hour Photo is a satisfying film ("raises more questions than it answers," my significant other said after we saw it). But thanks to Williams and Harris, both expose a scary kind of needfulness that we've all felt, at least a little.</fullReview>

		<username>Dehlia_</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Unsatisfying, but worth seeing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robin Williams plays Sy, a photo developer at a Target/K-Mart type of store who becomes obsessed with a family whose film he has been developing for over a decade. The movie was fascinating to watch, often in its small details. It spends a lot of time looking like a photograph, and seeing itself from inside itself, if you know what I mean. One of the most fascinating scenes was a complete throwaway. The basic premise is that Sy is this totally invisible guy. He's no one, a nebbish, and no one ever notices him. From the vantage point of his non-existence, he builds a dangerous and terrifying obsession; he uses his invisibility to stalk a family that is happily "normal," living on the other side of the invisibility divide. Gradually you can realize that we meet "Sy" all the time, and in this one, telling scene, Sy stops for dinner at a restaurant. The waitress knows him, asks after his family (whom she thinks is depicted in the photos he obsesses over), and so on. And it is there for the viewer to know that this waitress, too, is Sy. She is invisible to him, he doesn't see or notice her, but she knows him. Unfortunately, the story goes on to depend on more and more coincidences, both of unlikely circumstance and of converged timing, rather than staying in the viewpoint of ordinariness it has created. As one thing leads to another, it looks more like a movie and less like Sy working at Sav Mart, and that weakens the movie. Still, worth seeing. 8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>cj909</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>An artfully composed unsettling thriller.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The art direction and cinematography of this film is fantastic, and that is not a 'back-handed' compliment. In fact, the way the film is shot and directed is symbolic of the photograph-based fantasy world Sy lives in. I found the overall look of the film to be very effective; this is a man who looks at snapshots all day long and daydreams about a beautiful, artfully arranged life complete with a photogenic family but has little to no actual interaction with real people in a real and meaningful way. A small but particularly effective scene is that in which Sy is crouched in a corner looking at the photographs taken by his 'fantasy nephew' - all are of inanimate objects, cartoonish objects with happy painted smiles but nonetheless inanimate, no real people; his 'nephew' also avoids actual human contact (in Sy's mind.) The actual plot of the film is thrilling if not unique or particularly spellbinding, but to me the the subtle and not so subtle portrayals of Sy's incredibly removed and sterile existence/environment were the real treat here. Many of the shots reminded me of the iconic scene in the supermarket in 'Repo Man' where all the items within were generic, white labels with black block printing. I almost think this film works more as an art piece than a movie. Which is again, not a backhanded compliment. (and a P.S. - as a former photo lab tech, I'm wondering why Sy didn't record a number of those photos as mistakes to hide his tracks. Or what store has a photo lab roughly the size of an entire 7-11 with no garishly colored advertisements or fliers or sideline products all over the counter....)</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Sav-Mart is scary...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is something ugly and sterile, dehumanizing about places like the "Sav-mart" in this film (of which there are more and more every day back here in real life). I don't like stores like those. I'd rather go live on the streets than work in a place like that -- at least the streets still have color, a sense of humanity and I wouldn't have to turn up in a well lighted tomb every morning working with zombies for as little money as they can get away with paying. Against this cold, icy paragon of nightmarish 00's consumerism it's no surprise that Robin William's character would snap. Anybody could snap -- it's an apt place to go crazy in, as devoid of genuine human contact as a crypt. And when Williams meets Nielsen's character in a modern day mall (another such place) eating lunch and she says "Just fueling up to do more shopping..." (shudder).That's what this film is really about, in my opinion -- the alienation and cheap emptiness of today's consumer culture, and the lie of the "perfect" suburban family (whose beautiful looking house is made of cheap synthetic materials, no deeper or more real than the photos taken there). It's been done before, and certainly better than this... the script is mediocre to average, but the lighting, cinematography and acting are all well above average. A must-see for anyone who's felt lonely, alienated and set adrift in today's culture that turns you into a buying machine from cradle to grave... then it turns your relatives into coffin and funeral service consumers, and puts a supermall-bought set of clothes on your dead body and a copyrighted and trademarked quip on your headstone. Terrifying -- 7/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A reflection on loyalty</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A sad tale about a lonely man obsessed with one family (Robin Williams, more into Popeye's territory than Mrs. Doubtfire's) turns into a sensitive reflection on loyalty, solidarity and togetherness. It falls into the traps of clichéd psychological melodrama whenever it tries to explain the characters' motivations, but during the moments it touches delicate issues of commitment in love relationships, it becomes an intelligent movie. It is the more surprising because it shows a realization about being loyal to a partner done by a young director/scriptwriter, that most ordinary human beings like us realize in old age, sometimes too late. Not surprisingly this story has no blood, sexual molestation scenes or shootings, and it works most of the time.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Pretty good all things considered</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This was a pretty satisfying thriller, and an excellent departure for Robin Williams.Williams was a very good choice for two reasons. He really does look the part for one, and secondly because people are probably a little tired of seeing Williams play himself in role after role, a 60 year old man who acts like a 6 year old. His performance is very good, he does makes a good villain (is Sy really a villain?) but admittedly, it's at least partially novelty value and would wear off if he played these roles too often.The rest of the cast doesn't really deserve a mention. Connie Neilson is, as always, completely unremarkable and invisible. Which isn't necessarily a bad thing, it's much worse to play a role with limited requirements and look like you are trying to stand out.*** SPOILERS ***I really understood Sy completely. As a loner, you are deathly afraid of intruding into another person's life, but at the same time you desperately wish they would invite you into it of their own free will. But sadly, that never seems to happen, no matter how many opportunities you give them. The part where Sy is fired, you can really feel his pain and what the job means to him. It is all he has, and whether you think keeping private photos of others is so bad or not, you feel great sympathy for him. It's a very good piece of acting by Williams.I think the ending, while not being exactly predictable, was still pretty cliche. We learn Sy had been abused as a child and that his abusers had taken photographs of him being abused, and that was probably the catalyst for his obsession with photos of happy people. And that was why his punishment of Will was by ostensibly taking degrading pictures of him and the woman he cheated with.And speaking form Sy's perspective, there's really only one thing worse than seeing a man who has it all go and cheat. It's seeing his wife forgive him and take him back anyway. Having said that, I really find it hard to sympathise with women who marry men like Will. They really do want to have their cake and eat it too, meaning that they want a guy with the money and success to give them everything they want, and then complain that he's away working so much to provide those things. It's little wonder with that such men under these kinds of conflicting requirements end up seeking affection from a less demanding third party.One final thing I'd like to comment on was the score. The music tried too hard to manipulate the audience. It goes so brooding and dark when revealing Sy's wall of photos, as though it's a wall of kiddie porn or snuff or something. It's not, it's pictures of a happy family, the kind Sy always wished he could have for himself. Speaking of manipulation, I am not a big fan of scenes like the one where Sy is inside the Yorkin's house and about to be busted, but then we discover it was merely a fantasy. This kind of fake suspense and tension is really a cheap and tacky trick and has been used too often in movies lately. And it ruins what was a truly creepy and intense scene to discover none of it was real.Overall though, I enjoyed the movie and enjoyed William's performance. Definitely worth seeing.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Glimpses of excellence, overshadowed by a lifeless family</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>ONE HOUR PHOTO **Robin Williams is cast out of his normal territory in this quite bizarre and somewhat interesting movie. He plays a character called Sy who is a photo developer at a local supermarket who has obsessed about some of his clients for a number of years. Cut off from society in general and with no close relatives his only solace in life is the people he serves and the dedication to his job. But one family in particular appeals to him more than any other, and he soon obsesses to the extent where he follows the families footsteps, has a constant record of their photographs and wanders around their property. But when he gets fired from his job for misuse of the equipment he fills his time by delving even deeper into the families life, uncovering deceit and turmoil. Williams' performance is deeply disturbing and harrowing, because of the isolation Sy feels in a world where he sees so many faces in photographs in a day. Williams is wonderful in his part. The problem comes in not where he takes the movie or the movie's choice of plot and direction. We are supposed to feel sorry and fearful for the family he obsesses about, and we are supposed to be left aghast at what will happen next. The problem is that the family is so wooden and lifeless that I did not care what happened to them. And that for me is where it fell down. We get snapshots of the family, and hints of where their problems lie. We know there's something not quite right when Sy's intuition tells us so. But not enough detail went into the family for us to appreciate them and get to know them. Therefore when Williams' character torments them through his problem with isolation, it's just a prelude to a weird ending. A shame really because the film showed a lot of potential.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I saw this a few days after seeing The Ring, and while one is a horror movie and the other is more of a psychological thriller, Photo made me think of The Ring because of that same sense of quiet dread threaded through the movie. Beautifully conceived and executed, it's still Williams that makes this movie. It reminds that, in spite of way too many feel-good comedies, Williams can really act if he wants to, and here he is almost unrecognizable, perfectly capturing the hidden intensity of the protagonist with his quiet demeanor and stifled body language. There's one moment at the end that, while effective, struck me as a bit too pat, but overall this is a truly brilliant film, well worth seeing.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2003-10-12</date>
		<username>Snake-666</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Good, but not great.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robin Williams is Sy Parish, a seemingly pleasant man who works at the photograph processing department of a large discount store. His favourite customers are Nina Yorkin (Connie Nielsen) and her son Jake (Dylan Smith). Sy, however, has a rather unhealthy obsession with Nina and her family and often dreams of being a larger part of their world.?One Hour Photo' is an entertaining movie from director Mark Romanek. The movie avoids most common clichés and portrays Williams' character as a nice yet socially maladjusted man, rather than a sick stalker. Make no mistake, Sy Parish is a deeply disturbed man but the viewer is made to sympathise with him rather than detest him. Through some good storytelling we are able to see the development of Williams' unhealthy obsession and are made to question whether he is actually doing much wrong? It is certainly wrong to stalk someone the way he does, but at the end of the day he means little harm. Mark Romanek's screenplay does a rather good job of portraying this despite featuring quite a few moments of unnecessary and forced dialogue. ?One Hour Photo' is paced rather well, though it does seem to drag in places and more too quickly in others. However, the story that develops is an interesting one and stirring enough to keep the viewer watching.Williams is very credible in his role as the disturbed Sy Parish and performs up to a high-standard that we have come to expect from him. ?One Hour Photo' allows us to see the vastness of Robin's talent and his ability to fit into almost any role. While perhaps not his best role he is certainly the star of the show and delivers his lines surprisingly convincingly. I was wary of Robin Williams playing this sort of role but now realise that he is just as adept playing this type of character as any other. Connie Nielsen is also good as the unsuspecting Nina, though fails to really impress. Flaws with the script prevent Connie's character from really standing out. There is a noticeable lack of character-development in certain places and while most things are wrapped up at the end, some questions are left unanswered. The ending is very powerful and saves the few scenes before it which seemed to drag. When the reason and extent of Sy's sickness is unveiled the viewer is left with a compassionate and strangely hollow feeling.Apart from the final ten minutes there are few memorable moments (except a short but effective nightmare sequence), yet the movie is still rather fulfilling. Some proficient direction compensate for the intermittent flaws in the screenplay rather well. ?One Hour Photo' is an appealing and compelling thriller with few scares but a real psychological and unsettling edge. Williams, though not on top-form, really helps to make this movie and it was a role he desperately needed after a few years in obscurity. My only real complaints with 'One Hour Photo' are the lack of character development in places, the sporadic moments of unnecessary dialogue and infrequent screenplay flaws. I recommend this movie for at least one watch. My rating for ?One Hour Photo' ? 7/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-03-28</date>
		<username>TxMike</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>One more reason to abandon film and go to digital photography!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film explores the age-old question "does the end justify the means?" Sy the photo guy is a nice but lonely man, lives in a very sparse apartment and a hamster running in a cage. As the film opens we see him being photographed, just arrested by the stalking division. We wonder what Sy has done, how serious it is, and the rest of the film is a long flashback which explains how he got there. The critic Ebert has an accurate review. Good movie, one of Robin Williams' best roles, really good character study.SPOILERS follow - don't read until you've seen the movie, OK??Sy in his loneliness has been the photo tech at SavMart for 11 years, and during most of those has followed one family, essentially adopting them in his mind. The wife is one of his best customers, and he always makes an extra set of prints so he can keep a set, secretly of course. But his wall at home becomes his "family" photo album. He adores the wife and son, and when he meets up with the husband reacts almost like we might when accidentally coming across our favorite actor in the grocery store. He views them as the ideal family, and secretly fantasizes that he becomes part of their family, and is called "Uncle Sy."All that is shattered one day when he develops photos which shows the husband in romantic situations with a sexy female co-worker. Coincidentally, he is fired for overall suspicious performance, including several hundred unaccounted photos, and on his last day slips copies of the offending photos in the wife's set of prints. He further tracks down the husband at the hotel during one of his illicit trysts, and forces them to pose nude for photos of them in compromising positions. The police nab him and we get back to the scene which began the film. The film closes with a photo of Sy and his adopted family, everyone happy and smiling. On the DVD's director's commentary he said he hopes it is vague, so that you can interpret it either as a fantasy shot, or perhaps they really did mend fences and develop the relationship. However, it is clear from the same commentary track that Williams considers it just a fantasy in Sy's mind. That seems to me the best fit. In this role, Williams abandons all the acting quirks he is usually noted for, and adopts an entirely new persona for Sy. In this role he demonstrates why he is one of the overall best actors of his time. Good movie, good character study.The DVD is a good one, although the extras are rather ordinary. The sound track is especially good, setting the mood, and some of the deep bass literally is visceral.July 2012 edit: Just watched it again, still an excellent movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>(DARK) PORTRAITS A SPECIALTY</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>ONE HOUR PHOTO (2002) **** Robin Williams, Connie Nielsen, Michael Vartan, Gary Cole, Dylan Smith, Eriq LaSalle, Erin Daniels, Clark Gregg. Williams (in one of his best roles to date showing his true range as an actor) stars as a lonely manager of a film development center in a convenience store who becomes obsessed with a family whose pictures prove to be more than a thousand words. Conveying the pain, desire and utter despair of a disturbed man wrestling with the inner demons of a depleted life, Williams makes Sy Parrish an indelibly remarkable cinematic character along the lines of Travis Bickle, Norman Bates and Harry Caul . Mixed with creepy awareness and an acute social commentary by writer/director Mark Romanek (in a notable debut) who knowingly captures genuine moments of unease and empathy in a particularly conceived concept: how does one live without human contact and the essential ingredient of love? A stunning achievement by all connected with one of the year's best films.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>My Experiment</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had written a negative review of ONE HOUR PHOTO a couple of years ago when it came out and then regretted writing it because I've since undertaken a more studied approach to films. So, I thought, I'll delete that comment and watch it again in the spirit of anticipating artistry. I'll be more charitable, I thought, see things that I missed before; I'll come out a fan. Of course, you know from my heavy-handed foreshadowing (borrowing one of the director's approaches) that it never happened. I like this film much less than I did the first time.To be fair, there are some successful aspects of this enterprise. At one point I noted that the film technique far surpasses the story. The movie is slick-looking, indeed. The use of color is rampant, albeit largely meaningless. The lighting is affective and needs desperately to be tied to a story and a character. The acting never caught my attention which is a good thing.But the movie is so painfully plodding that I thought at one point that the filmmaker must be a famous person's son who had a lot of money and a lot of Hollywood ties (look at the cast--even the day roles are names) and a nebulous idea that he ultimately couldn't pull off. I haven't yet investigated that theory but I did read that this is a former music video director. That explains a lot--visuals without meaning. This movie is contrived, manipulative, and follows all of the Hollywood clichés--it just LOOKS like it doesn't. One can only guess at the positive critical responses, but I'll bet you that none of the critics watched it a second time.This movie reminds me of smart people who want to write but who don't read. They're capable, perhaps, but nearly knowledge-free of the greatness in their chosen medium. As one IMDb user put it (and I'm sorry I can't remember who) "art isn't easy."</fullReview>

		<username>moonspinner55</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Uncertain development, murky returns...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Wimpering suspense-drama with variable performances and writing. Robin Williams is nearly unrecognizable as a friendless photo developer in a shopping center whose not-so-innocent obsession with a seemingly happy family leads to tragedy. I'm not sure why 'lonely' always equals 'sociopathic' in these kinds of movies--if it's for dramatic content, "One Hour Photo" comes up doubly short, as there isn't much substance in this script nor is there an emotional pay-off at the finale. Williams, his lips tightened into a grimace, displays a convincingly false happy-front; with his alert eyes and quick little movements, he gives this character more depth than writer-director Mark Romanek probably envisioned (it's certainly not there in the script). Romanek is very flashy, giving us canny snippets of character quirks, but he is neither interested in being enlightening (except for a brief treatise on the history of photography) nor entertaining, and the fantasies and dream sequences are poor excuses for narrative content. ** from ****</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2002-10-10</date>
		<username>tedg (tedg@filmsfolded.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Negatives</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers herein.I dread the Robin Williams projects. Its not so much him, as long as he doesn't give us any more grieving husband retreads. No, it is all the fuss that is made around him to focus on an exaggerate his apparent quirkiness. The man does know a few things about energy and how it affects the engine of self. But most of what we get out of his characters comes from others: the lighting, makeup, design, strange notes in the script, even adaptive reactions by other actors. To see how powerful this effect is, pay attention to Williams' voiceover as narrator. See how ordinary it is, how inapt it is at conveying what is intended, how poor the rhythm. Now contrast that with his on-screen presence, where the world is engineered to support him. Striking.But in this case, the interesting thing isn't the story or the acting, nor any of that. It is the example, yet another one, of a growing acceptance in mainstream film of the notion of explicit self-reference. That's where we have a collection of images made about the making of a collection of images, usually film or photography. (Another thread are performances about performances.)In such self-reference, the voyeurism of the character is folded into our own voyeurism in watching what is going on. A clever writer will trick us into believing the character to be right until the end where he and us are revealed for what we are. And so we have here. I remark that the self-reference is particularly explicit, but in this case not particularly well done, because no moral ambiguity is introduced. I understand that such a thing was part of the original project, but as with all Hollywood efforts now, test audiences and `protect-the-investment' strategies washed it all out.Ted's Evaluation -- 2 of 4: Has some interesting elements.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>yawn</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>people here keep mentioning hitchcock in regard to this film, who they should be referencing is michael powell, whose 'peeping tom' is the film being more directly addressed. or ripped off, however you like to look at it. thematically it's much much closer to the powell than to any hitchcock, but i digress.i found this film to be a painfully boring and derivative one.the color palette--and yes, i did just use that phrase--in the film drove me insane. it was obvious and silly in the extreme. sy gets cold alienating blue tone while the yorkin family gets a warm, happy tone. it was one of the first things i noticed about the film and it bugged me tremendously. color timing, when done well, can be a subtle indicator of psychological or emotional states, but this was not subtle. it practically screamed at you, and my response to said screaming was 'okay, okay! i get it!' the performances are not what i'd call amazing in any way. i found myself cringing at robin williams' portrayal of sy, and i felt sorry for the actor, not the character. this would tend to go against the grain for me since i usually enjoy this sort of character--travis bickle, norman bates, what have you. i couldn't muster any sympathy for sy at all.his lapse into psychosis takes place with the two characters that one feels the least about, so the emotional impact isn't there. i didn't really care about what happened to them and i doubt most people really felt any immediate concern for them either.two things i did enjoy about this film--sy's weird fantasies/daydreams/nightmares and the ending, which i found to be quite curious. it didn't make sense in the context of the film, but i still enjoyed it. make of that what you will.</fullReview>

And HOW sensitive of this 9-year-old.Why didn't the wife confront the husband?The real problem with this movie is that there isn't enough material here to sustain a feature length film. 		<date>2005-03-17</date>
		<username>Jackson Booth-Millard</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>One Hour Photo</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of the best and most creepy thrillers I have ever seen. Robin Williams plays another great non-comedy role (just like Insomnia) as Sy Parrish, a knowledgeable photo developer at a local supermarket. He has been developing the photographs of the Yorkin family, from the films given by mother and wife Nina Yorkin (Connie Nielsen) since young Jake (Dylan Smith) was a baby. What they and everyone else doesn't know is that at his house his wall is covered with this family's photos! He basically wants to be part of this family, even though he knows he can't, so you can slightly sympathise with his loneliness. When the supermarket manager Bill Owens (Gary Cole) finds out fires him for theft, he has one more week at work, so he starts having a breakdown because he won't "see" the family anymore. But then it gets worse, in his last developing he discovers the father and husband of the family Will Yorkin (Michael Vartan) has been having an affair with Maya Burson (Erin Daniels), through some photos that he finds. So he exacts angry revenge by first slipping this affair photo in the family's photo envelope, and then he finds where the Dad and this other woman hang out. When he finds them at the hotel room, he threatens to stab them (with a stolen knife) if they do not pose in pornographic poses (and smile), this is the most uncomfortable scene. In the end, he gets arrested for stalking the manager's daughter and the final shot is him in a Yorkin family photo, but is it his imagination or reality? Also starring Paul Hansen Kim as Yoshi Araki, Eriq La Salle as Detective James Van Der Zee and Clark Gregg as Detective Paul Outerbridge. This is what I like to refer to as the nut-case film, like Falling Down, Fatal Attraction and Misery. Robin Williams was number 19 on The 100 Greatest Movie Stars. Very good!</fullReview>
	</review>
